from ill-humour, from malignancy.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1178">
Text = Well, in all these recognitions and
disgraces it is that there lies a voluptuous pleasure.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1179">
Text = As though he
would say: "I am worrying you, I am lacerating your hearts, I am keeping
every one in the house awake.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1180">
Text = Well, stay awake then, you, too, feel
every minute that I have toothache.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1181">
Text = I am not a hero to you now, as I
tried to seem before, but simply a nasty person, an impostor.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1182">
Text = Well, so
be it, then!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1183">
Text = I am very glad that you see through me.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1184">
Text = It is nasty for you
to hear my despicable moans: well, let it be nasty; here I will let you
have a nastier flourish in a minute...." You do not understand even now,
gentlemen?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1185">
Text = No, it seems our development and our consciousness must go
further to understand all the intricacies of this pleasure.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1186">
Text = You laugh?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1187">
Text = Delighted.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1188">
Text = My jests, gentlemen, are of course in bad taste, jerky,
involved, lacking self-confidence.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1189">
Text = But of course that is because I do
not respect myself.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1190">
Text = Can a man of perception respect himself at all?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1191">
Text = V
</Sent>
<Sent id="1192">
Text = Come, can a man who attempts to find enjoyment in the very feeling of
his own degradation possibly have a spark of respect for himself?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1193">
Text = I am
not saying this now from any mawkish kind of remorse.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1194">
Text = And, indeed, I
could never endure saying, "Forgive me, Papa, I won't do it again," not
because I am incapable of saying that--on the contrary, perhaps just
because I have been too capable of it, and in what a way, too!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1195">
Text = As though
of design I used to get into trouble in cases when I was not to blame in
any way.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1196">
Text = That was the nastiest part of it.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1197">
Text = At the same time I was
genuinely touched and penitent, I used to shed tears and, of course,
deceived myself, though I was not acting in the least and there was a
sick feeling in my heart at the time....
</Sent>
<Sent id="1198">
Text = For that one could not blame
even the laws of nature, though the laws of nature have continually all
my life offended me more than anything.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1199">
Text = It is loathsome to remember it
all, but it was loathsome even then.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1200">
Text = Of course, a minute or so later I
would realize wrathfully that it was all a lie, a revolting lie, an
affected lie, that is, all this penitence, this emotion, these vows of
reform.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1201">
Text = You will ask why did I worry myself with such antics: answer,
because it was very dull to sit with one's hands folded, and so one
began cutting capers.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1202">
Text = That is really it.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1203">
Text = Observe yourselves more
carefully, gentlemen, then you will understand that it is so.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1204">
Text = I invented
adventures for myself and made up a life, so as at least to live in some
way.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1205">
Text = How many times it has happened to me--well, for instance, to take
offence simply on purpose, for nothing; and one knows oneself, of
course, that one is offended at nothing, that one is putting it on, but
yet one brings oneself, at last to the point of being really offended.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1206">
Text = All my life I have had an impulse to play such pranks, so that in the
end I could not control it in myself.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1207">
Text = Another time, twice, in fact, I
tried hard to be in love.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1208">
Text = I suffered, too, gentlemen, I assure you.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1209">
Text = In
the depth of my heart there was no faith in my suffering, only a faint
stir of mockery, but yet I did suffer, and in the real, orthodox way;
</Sent>
<Sent id="1210">
Text = I
was jealous, beside myself ... and it was all from _ennui_, gentlemen,
all from _ennui_; inertia overcame me.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1211">
Text = You know the direct, legitimate
fruit of consciousness is inertia, that is, conscious
sitting-with-the-hands-folded.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1212">
Text = I have referred to this already.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1213">
Text = I
repeat, I repeat with emphasis: all "direct" persons and men of action
are active just because they are stupid and limited.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1214">
Text = How explain that?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1215">
Text = I
will tell you: in consequence of their limitation they take immediate
and secondary causes for primary ones, and in that way persuade
themselves more quickly and easily than other people do that they have
found an infallible foundation for their activity, and their minds are
at ease and you know that is the chief thing.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1216">
Text = To begin to act, you know,
you must first have your mind completely at ease and no trace of doubt
left in it.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1217">
Text = Why, how am I, for example to set my mind at rest?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1218">
Text = Where are
the primary causes on which I am to build?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1219">
Text = Where are my foundations?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1220">
Text = Where am I to get them from?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1221">
Text = I exercise myself in reflection, and
consequently with me every primary cause at once draws after itself
another still more primary, and so on to infinity.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1222">
Text = That is just the
essence of every sort of consciousness and reflection.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1223">
Text = It must be a case
of the laws of nature again.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1224">
Text = What is the result of it in the end?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1225">
Text = Why,
just the same.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1226">
Text = Remember I spoke just now of vengeance. (I am sure you
did not take it in.) I said that a man revenges himself because he sees
justice in it.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1227">
Text = Therefore he has found a primary cause, that is, justice.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1228">
Text = And so he is at rest on all sides, and consequently he carries out his
revenge calmly and successfully, being persuaded that he is doing a just
and honest thing.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1229">
Text = But I see no justice in it, I find no sort of virtue
in it either, and consequently if I attempt to revenge myself, it is
only out of spite.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1230">
Text = Spite, of course, might overcome everything, all my
doubts, and so might serve quite successfully in place of a primary
cause, precisely because it is not a cause.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1231">
Text = But what is to be done if I
have not even spite (I began with that just now, you know)?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1232">
Text = In
consequence again of those accursed laws of consciousness, anger in me
is subject to chemical disintegration.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1233">
Text = You look into it, the object
flies off into air, your reasons evaporate, the criminal is not to be
found, the wrong becomes not a wrong but a phantom, something like the
toothache, for which no one is to blame, and consequently there is only
the same outlet left again--that is, to beat the wall as hard as you
can.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1234">
Text = So you give it up with a wave of the hand because you have not
found a fundamental cause.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1235">
Text = And try letting yourself be carried away by
your feelings, blindly, without reflection, without a primary cause,
repelling consciousness at least for a time; hate or love, if only not
to sit with your hands folded.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1236">
Text = The day after to-morrow, at the latest,
you will begin despising yourself for having knowingly deceived
yourself.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1237">
Text = Result: a soap-bubble and inertia.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1238">
Text = Oh, gentlemen, do you know,
perhaps I consider myself an intelligent man, only because all my life I
have been able neither to begin nor to finish anything.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1239">
Text = Granted I am a
babbler, a harmless vexatious babbler, like all of us.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1240">
Text = But what is to be
done if the direct and sole vocation of every intelligent man is babble,
that is, the intentional pouring of water through a sieve?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1241">
Text = VI
</Sent>
<Sent id="1242">
Text = Oh, if I had done nothing simply from laziness!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1243">
Text = Heavens, how I should
have respected myself, then.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1244">
Text = I should have respected myself because I
should at least have been capable of being lazy; there would at least
have been one quality, as it were, positive in me, in which I could have
believed myself.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1245">
Text = Question: What is he?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1246">
Text = Answer: A sluggard; how very
pleasant it would have been to hear that of oneself!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1247">
Text = It would mean that
I was positively defined, it would mean that there was something to say
about me. "Sluggard"--why, it is a calling and vocation, it is a career.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1248">
Text = Do not jest, it is so.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1249">
Text = I should then be a member of the best club by
right, and should find my occupation in continually respecting myself.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1250">
Text = I
knew a gentleman who prided himself all his life on being a connoisseur
of Lafitte.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1251">
Text = He considered this as his positive virtue, and never doubted
himself.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1252">
Text = He died, not simply with a tranquil, but with a triumphant,
conscience, and he was quite right, too.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1253">
Text = Then I should have chosen a
career for myself, I should have been a sluggard and a glutton, not a
simple one, but, for instance, one with sympathies for everything good
and beautiful.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1254">
Text = How do you like that?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1255">
Text = I have long had visions of it.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1256">
Text = That
"good and beautiful" weighs heavily on my mind at forty.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1257">
Text = But that is at
forty; then--oh, then it would have been different!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1258">
Text = I should have found
for myself a form of activity in keeping with it, to be precise,
drinking to the health of everything "good and beautiful." I should have
snatched at every opportunity to drop a tear into my glass and then to
drain it to all that is "good and beautiful." I should then have turned
everything into the good and the beautiful; in the nastiest,
unquestionable trash, I should have sought out the good and the
beautiful.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1259">
Text = I should have exuded tears like a wet sponge.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1260">
Text = An artist, for
instance, paints a picture worthy of Gay.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1261">
Text = At once I drink to the health
of the artist who painted the picture worthy of Gay, because I love all
that is "good and beautiful." An author has written _As you will_: at
once I drink to the health of "any one you will" because I love all that
is "good and beautiful."
</Sent>
<Sent id="1262">
Text = I should claim respect for doing so.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1263">
Text = I should persecute any one who
would not show me respect.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1264">
Text = I should live at ease, I should die with
dignity, why, it is charming, perfectly charming!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1265">
Text = And what a good round
belly I should have grown, what a treble chin I should have established,
what a ruby nose I should have coloured for myself, so that every one
would have said, looking at me: "Here is an asset!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1266">
Text = Here is something
real and solid!" And, say what you like, it is very agreeable to hear
such remarks about oneself in this negative age.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1267">
Text = VII
</Sent>
<Sent id="1268">
Text = But these are all golden dreams.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1269">
Text = Oh, tell me, who was it first
announced, who was it first proclaimed, that man only does nasty things
because he does not know his own interests; and that if he were
enlightened, if his eyes were opened to his real normal interests, man
would at once cease to do nasty things, would at once become good and
noble because, being enlightened and understanding his real advantage,
he would see his own advantage in the good and nothing else, and we all
know that not one man can, consciously, act against his own interests,
consequently, so to say, through necessity, he would begin doing good?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1270">
Text = Oh, the babe!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1271">
Text = Oh, the pure, innocent child!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1272">
Text = Why, in the first place,
when in all these thousands of years has there been a time when man has
acted only from his own interest?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1273">
Text = What is to be done with the millions
of facts that bear witness that men, _consciously_, that is fully
understanding their real interests, have left them in the background and
have rushed headlong on another path, to meet peril and danger,
compelled to this course by nobody and by nothing, but, as it were,
simply disliking the beaten track, and have obstinately, wilfully,
struck out another difficult, absurd way, seeking it almost in the
darkness.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1274">
Text = So, I suppose, this obstinacy and perversity were pleasanter
to them than any advantage....
</Sent>
<Sent id="1275">
Text = Advantage!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1276">
Text = What is advantage?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1277">
Text = And will
you take it upon yourself to define with perfect accuracy in what the
advantage of man consists?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1278">
Text = And what if it so happens that a man's
advantage, _sometimes_, not only may, but even must, consist in his
desiring in certain cases what is harmful to himself and not
advantageous?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1279">
Text = And if so, if there can be such a case, the whole
principle falls into dust.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1280">
Text = What do you think--are there such cases?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1281">
Text = You
laugh; laugh away, gentlemen, but only answer me: have man's advantages
been reckoned up with perfect certainty?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1282">
Text = Are there not some which not
only have not been included but cannot possibly be included under any
classification?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1283">
Text = You see, you gentlemen have, to the best of my
knowledge, taken your whole register of human advantages from the
averages of statistical figures and politico-economical formulas.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1284">
Text = Your
advantages are prosperity, wealth, freedom, peace--and so on, and so on.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1285">
Text = So that the man who should, for instance, go openly and knowingly in
opposition to all that list would, to your thinking, and indeed mine,
too, of course, be an obscurantist or an absolute madman: would not he?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1286">
Text = But, you know, this is what is surprising: why does it so happen that
all these statisticians, sages and lovers of humanity, when they reckon
up human advantages invariably leave out one?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1287">
Text = They don't even take it
into their reckoning in the form in which it should be taken, and the
whole reckoning depends upon that.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1288">
Text = It would be no great matter, they
would simply have to take it, this advantage, and add it to the list.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1289">
Text = But the trouble is, that this strange advantage does not fall under any
classification and is not in place in any list.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1290">
Text = I have a friend for
instance....
</Sent>
<Sent id="1291">
Text = Ech! gentlemen, but of course he is your friend, too; and
indeed there is no one, no one, to whom he is not a friend!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1292">
Text = When he
prepares for any undertaking this gentleman immediately explains to you,
elegantly and clearly, exactly how he must act in accordance with the
laws of reason and truth.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1293">
Text = What is more, he will talk to you with
excitement and passion of the true normal interests of man; with irony
he will upbraid the shortsighted fools who do not understand their own
interests, nor the true significance of virtue; and, within a quarter of
an hour, without any sudden outside provocation, but simply through
something inside him which is stronger than all his interests, he will
go off on quite a different tack--that is, act in direct opposition to
what he has just been saying about himself, in opposition to the laws of
reason, in opposition to his own advantage, in fact in opposition to
everything....
</Sent>
<Sent id="1294">
Text = I warn you that my friend is a compound personality, and
therefore it is difficult to blame him as an individual.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1295">
Text = The fact is,
gentlemen, it seems there must really exist something that is dearer to
almost every man than his greatest advantages, or (not to be illogical)
there is a most advantageous advantage (the very one omitted of which we
spoke just now) which is more important and more advantageous than all
other advantages, for the sake of which a man if necessary is ready to
act in opposition to all laws; that is, in opposition to reason, honour,
peace, prosperity--in fact, in opposition to all those excellent and
useful things if only he can attain that fundamental, most advantageous
advantage which is dearer to him than all. "Yes, but it's advantage all
the same" you will retort.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1296">
Text = But excuse me, I'll make the point clear, and
it is not a case of playing upon words.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1297">
Text = What matters is, that this
advantage is remarkable from the very fact that it breaks down all our
classifications, and continually shatters every system constructed by
lovers of mankind for the benefit of mankind.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1298">
Text = In fact, it upsets
everything.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1299">
Text = But before I mention this advantage to you, I want to
compromise myself personally, and therefore I boldly declare that all
these fine systems, all these theories for explaining to mankind their
real normal interests, in order that inevitably striving to pursue these
interests they may at once become good and noble--are, in my opinion, so
far, mere logical exercises!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1300">
Text = Yes, logical exercises.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1301">
Text = Why, to maintain
this theory of the regeneration of mankind by means of the pursuit of
his own advantage is to my mind almost the same thing as ... as to
affirm, for instance, following Buckle, that through civilization
mankind becomes softer, and consequently less bloodthirsty and less
fitted for warfare.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1302">
Text = Logically it does seem to follow from his arguments.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1303">
Text = But man has such a predilection for systems and abstract deductions that
he is ready to distort the truth intentionally, he is ready to deny the
evidence of his senses only to justify his logic.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1304">
Text = I take this example
because it is the most glaring instance of it.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1305">
Text = Only look about you:
blood is being spilt in streams, and in the merriest way, as though it
were champagne.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1306">
Text = Take the whole of the nineteenth century in which Buckle
lived.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1307">
Text = Take Napoleon--the Great and also the present one.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1308">
Text = Take North
America--the eternal union.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1309">
Text = Take the farce of Schleswig-Holstein....
</Sent>
<Sent id="1310">
Text = And
what is it that civilization softens in us?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1311">
Text = The only gain of
civilization for mankind is the greater capacity for variety of
sensations--and absolutely nothing more.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1312">
Text = And through the development of
this many-sidedness man may come to finding enjoyment in bloodshed.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1313">
Text = In
fact, this has already happened to him.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1314">
Text = Have you noticed that it is the
most civilized gentlemen who have been the subtlest slaughterers, to
whom the Attilas and Stenka Razins could not hold a candle, and if they
are not so conspicuous as the Attilas and Stenka Razins it is simply
because they are so often met with, are so ordinary and have become so
familiar to us.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1315">
Text = In any case civilization has made mankind if not more
bloodthirsty, at least more vilely, more loathsomely bloodthirsty.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1316">
Text = In
old days he saw justice in bloodshed and with his conscience at peace
exterminated those he thought proper.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1317">
Text = Now we do think bloodshed
abominable and yet we engage in this abomination, and with more energy
than ever.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1318">
Text = Which is worse?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1319">
Text = Decide that for yourselves.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1320">
Text = They say that
Cleopatra (excuse an instance from Roman history) was fond of sticking
gold pins into her slave-girls' breasts and derived gratification from
their screams and writhings.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1321">
Text = You will say that that was in the
comparatively barbarous times; that these are barbarous times too,
because also, comparatively speaking, pins are stuck in even now; that
though man has now learned to see more clearly than in barbarous ages,
he is still far from having learnt to act as reason and science would
dictate.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1322">
Text = But yet you are fully convinced that he will be sure to learn
when he gets rid of certain old bad habits, and when common sense and
science have completely re-educated human nature and turned it in a
normal direction.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1323">
Text = You are confident that then man will cease from
_intentional_ error and will, so to say, be compelled not to want to set
his will against his normal interests.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1324">
Text = That is not all; then, you say,
science itself will teach man (though to my mind it's a superfluous
luxury) that he never has really had any caprice or will of his own, and
that he himself is something of the nature of a piano-key or the stop of
an organ, and that there are, besides, things called the laws of nature;
so that everything he does is not done by his willing it, but is done of
itself, by the laws of nature.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1325">
Text = Consequently we have only to discover
these laws of nature, and man will no longer have to answer for his
actions and life will become exceedingly easy for him.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1326">
Text = All human actions
will then, of course, be tabulated according to these laws,
mathematically, like tables of logarithms up to 108,000, and entered in
an index; or, better still, there would be published certain edifying
works of the nature of encyclopædic lexicons, in which everything will
be so clearly calculated and explained that there will be no more
incidents or adventures in the world.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1327">
Text = Then--this is all what you say--new economic relations will be
established, all ready-made and worked out with mathematical exactitude,
so that every possible question will vanish in the twinkling of an eye,
simply because every possible answer to it will be provided.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1328">
Text = Then the
"Palace of Crystal" will be built.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1329">
Text = Then....
</Sent>
<Sent id="1330">
Text = In fact, those will be
halcyon days.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1331">
Text = Of course there is no guaranteeing (this is my comment)
that it will not be, for instance, frightfully dull then (for what will
one have to do when everything will be calculated and tabulated?), but on
the other hand everything will be extraordinarily rational.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1332">
Text = Of course
boredom may lead you to anything.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1333">
Text = It is boredom sets one sticking golden
pins into people, but all that would not matter.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1334">
Text = What is bad (this is my
comment again) is that I dare say people will be thankful for the gold
pins then.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1335">
Text = Man is stupid, you know, phenomenally stupid; or rather he is
not at all stupid, but he is so ungrateful that you could not find
another like him in all creation.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1336">
Text = I, for instance, would not be in the
least surprised if all of a sudden, _à propos_ of nothing, in the midst
of general prosperity a gentleman with an ignoble, or rather with a
reactionary and ironical, countenance were to arise and, putting his
arms akimbo, say to us all: "I say, gentlemen, hadn't we better kick
over the whole show and scatter rationalism to the winds, simply to send
these logarithms to the devil, and to enable us to live once more at our
own sweet foolish will!" That again would not matter; but what is
annoying is that he would be sure to find followers--such is the nature
of man.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1337">
Text = And all that for the most foolish reason, which, one would
think, was hardly worth mentioning: that is, that man everywhere and at
all times, whoever he may be, has preferred to act as he chose and not
in the least as his reason and advantage dictated.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1338">
Text = And one may choose
what is contrary to one's own interests, and sometimes one _positively
ought_ (that is my idea).
</Sent>
<Sent id="1339">
Text = One's own free unfettered choice, one's own
caprice, however wild it may be, one's own fancy worked up at times to
frenzy--is that very "most advantageous advantage" which we have
overlooked, which comes under no classification and against which all
systems and theories are continually being shattered to atoms.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1340">
Text = And how
do these wiseacres know that man wants a normal, a virtuous choice?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1341">
Text = What
has made them conceive that man must want a rationally advantageous
choice?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1342">
Text = What man wants is simply _independent_ choice, whatever that
independence may cost and wherever it may lead.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1343">
Text = And choice, of course,
the devil only knows what choice....
</Sent>
<Sent id="1344">
Text = VIII

"Ha! ha! ha!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1345">
Text = But you know there is no such thing as choice in reality,
say what you like," you will interpose with a chuckle. "Science has
succeeded in so far analysing man that we know already that choice and
what is called freedom of will is nothing else than----"
</Sent>
<Sent id="1346">
Text = Stay, gentlemen, I meant to begin with that myself.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1347">
Text = I confess, I was
rather frightened.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1348">
Text = I was just going to say that the devil only knows
what choice depends on, and that perhaps that was a very good thing, but
I remembered the teaching of science ... and pulled myself up.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1349">
Text = And here
you have begun upon it.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1350">
Text = Indeed, if there really is some day discovered a
formula for all our desires and caprices--that is, an explanation of
what they depend upon, by what laws they arise, how they develop, what
they are aiming at in one case and in another and so on, that is a real
mathematical formula--then, most likely, man will at once cease to feel
desire, indeed, he will be certain to.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1351">
Text = For who would want to choose by
rule?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1352">
Text = Besides, he will at once be transformed from a human being into an
organ-stop or something of the sort; for what is a man without desires,
without free will and without choice, if not a stop in an organ?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1353">
Text = What do
you think?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1354">
Text = Let us reckon the chances--can such a thing happen or not?

"H'm!" you decide. "Our choice is usually mistaken from a false view of
our advantage.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1355">
Text = We sometimes choose absolute nonsense because in our
foolishness we see in that nonsense the easiest means for attaining a
supposed advantage.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1356">
Text = But when all that is explained and worked out on
paper (which is perfectly possible, for it is contemptible and senseless
to suppose that some laws of nature man will never understand), then
certainly so-called desires will no longer exist.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1357">
Text = For if a desire should
come into conflict with reason we shall then reason and not desire,
because it will be impossible retaining our reason to be _senseless_ in
our desires, and in that way knowingly act against reason and desire to
injure ourselves.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1358">
Text = And as all choice and reasoning can be really
calculated--because there will some day be discovered the laws of our
so-called free will--so, joking apart, there may one day be something
like a table constructed of them, so that we really shall choose in
accordance with it.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1359">
Text = If, for instance, some day they calculate and prove
to me that I made a long nose at some one because I could not help
making a long nose at him and that I had to do it in that particular
way, what _freedom_ is left me, especially if I am a learned man and
have taken my degree somewhere?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1360">
Text = Then I should be able to calculate my
whole life for thirty years beforehand.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1361">
Text = In short, if this could be
arranged there would be nothing left for us to do; anyway, we should
have to understand that.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1362">
Text = And, in fact, we ought unwearyingly to repeat
to ourselves that at such and such a time and in such and such
circumstances nature does not ask our leave; that we have got to take
her as she is and not fashion her to suit our fancy, and if we really
aspire to formulas and tables of rules, and well, even ... to the
chemical retort, there's no help for it, we must accept the retort too,
or else it will be accepted without our consent...."
</Sent>
<Sent id="1363">
Text = Yes, but here I come to a stop!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1364">
Text = Gentlemen, you must excuse me for being
over-philosophical; it's the result of forty years underground!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1365">
Text = Allow me
to indulge my fancy.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1366">
Text = You see, gentlemen, reason is an excellent thing,
there's no disputing that, but reason is nothing but reason and
satisfies only the rational side of man's nature, while will is a
manifestation of the whole life, that is, of the whole human life
including reason and all the impulses.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1367">
Text = And although our life, in this
manifestation of it, is often worthless, yet it is life and not simply
extracting square roots.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1368">
Text = Here I, for instance, quite naturally want to
live, in order to satisfy all my capacities for life, and not simply my
capacity for reasoning, that is, not simply one twentieth of my capacity
for life.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1369">
Text = What does reason know?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1370">
Text = Reason only knows what it has succeeded
in learning (some things, perhaps, it will never learn; this is a poor
comfort, but why not say so frankly?) and human nature acts as a whole,
with everything that is in it, consciously or unconsciously, and, even
if it goes wrong, it lives.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1371">
Text = I suspect, gentlemen, that you are looking
at me with compassion; you tell me again that an enlightened and
developed man, such, in short, as the future man will be, cannot
consciously desire anything disadvantageous to himself, that that can be
proved mathematically.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1372">
Text = I thoroughly agree, it can--by mathematics.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1373">
Text = But I
repeat for the hundredth time, there is one case, one only, when man may
consciously, purposely, desire what is injurious to himself, what is
stupid, very stupid--simply in order to have the right to desire for
himself even what is very stupid and not to be bound by an obligation to
desire only what is sensible.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1374">
Text = Of course, this very stupid thing, this
caprice of ours, may be in reality, gentlemen, more advantageous for us
than anything else on earth, especially in certain cases.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1375">
Text = And in
particular it may be more advantageous than any advantage even when it
does us obvious harm, and contradicts the soundest conclusions of our
reason concerning our advantage--for in any circumstances it preserves
for us what is most precious and most important--that is, our
personality, our individuality.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1376">
Text = Some, you see, maintain that this really
is the most precious thing for mankind; choice can, of course, if it
chooses, be in agreement with reason; and especially if this be not
abused but kept within bounds.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1377">
Text = It is profitable and sometimes even
praiseworthy.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1378">
Text = But very often, and even most often, choice is utterly and
stubbornly opposed to reason ... and ... and ... do you know that that,
too, is profitable, sometimes even praiseworthy?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1379">
Text = Gentlemen, let us
suppose that man is not stupid. (Indeed one cannot refuse to suppose
that, if only from the one consideration, that, if man is stupid, then
who is wise?) But if he is not stupid, he is monstrously ungrateful!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1380">
Text = Phenomenally ungrateful.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1381">
Text = In fact, I believe that the best definition of
man is the ungrateful biped.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1382">
Text = But that is not all, that is not his worst
defect; his worst defect is his perpetual moral obliquity,
perpetual--from the days of the Flood to the Schleswig-Holstein period.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1383">
Text = Moral obliquity and consequently lack of good sense; for it has long
been accepted that lack of good sense is due to no other cause than
moral obliquity.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1384">
Text = Put it to the test and cast your eyes upon the history
of mankind.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1385">
Text = What will you see?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1386">
Text = Is it a grand spectacle?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1387">
Text = Grand, if you
like.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1388">
Text = Take the Colossus of Rhodes, for instance, that's worth something.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1389">
Text = With good reason Mr. Anaevsky testifies of it that some say that it is
the work of man's hands, while others maintain that it has been created
by nature herself.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1390">
Text = Is it many-coloured?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1391">
Text = May be it is many-coloured, too:
if one takes the dress uniforms, military and civilian, of all peoples
in all ages--that alone is worth something, and if you take the undress
uniforms you will never get to the end of it; no historian would be
equal to the job.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1392">
Text = Is it monotonous?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1393">
Text = May be it's monotonous too: it's
fighting and fighting; they are fighting now, they fought first and they
fought last--you will admit, that it is almost too monotonous.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1394">
Text = In short,
one may say anything about the history of the world--anything that might
enter the most disordered imagination.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1395">
Text = The only thing one can't say is
that it's rational.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1396">
Text = The very word sticks in one's throat.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1397">
Text = And, indeed,
this is the odd thing that is continually happening: there are
continually turning up in life moral and rational persons, sages and
lovers of humanity who make it their object to live all their lives as
morally and rationally as possible, to be, so to speak, a light to their
neighbours simply in order to show them that it is possible to live
morally and rationally in this world.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1398">
Text = And yet we all know that those
very people sooner or later have been false to themselves, playing some
queer trick, often a most unseemly one.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1399">
Text = Now I ask you: what can be
expected of man since he is a being endowed with such strange qualities?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1400">
Text = Shower upon him every earthly blessing, drown him in a sea of happiness,
so that nothing but bubbles of bliss can be seen on the surface; give
him economic prosperity, such that he should have nothing else to do but
sleep, eat cakes and busy himself with the continuation of his species,
and even then out of sheer ingratitude, sheer spite, man would play you
some nasty trick.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1401">
Text = He would even risk his cakes and would deliberately
desire the most fatal rubbish, the most uneconomical absurdity, simply
to introduce into all this positive good sense his fatal fantastic
element.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1402">
Text = It is just his fantastic dreams, his vulgar folly that he will
desire to retain, simply in order to prove to himself--as though that
were so necessary--that men still are men and not the keys of a piano,
which the laws of nature threaten to control so completely that soon one
will be able to desire nothing but by the calendar.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1403">
Text = And that is not all:
even if man really were nothing but a piano-key, even if this were
proved to him by natural science and mathematics, even then he would not
become reasonable, but would purposely do something perverse out of
simple ingratitude, simply to gain his point.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1404">
Text = And if he does not find
means he will contrive destruction and chaos, will contrive sufferings
of all sorts, only to gain his point!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1405">
Text = He will launch a curse upon the
world, and as only man can curse (it is his privilege, the primary
distinction between him and other animals), may be by his curse alone he
will attain his object--that is, convince himself that he is a man and
not a piano-key!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1406">
Text = If you say that all this, too, can be calculated and
tabulated--chaos and darkness and curses, so that the mere possibility
of calculating it all beforehand would stop it all, and reason would
reassert itself, then man would purposely go mad in order to be rid of
reason and gain his point!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1407">
Text = I believe in it, I answer for it, for the
whole work of man really seems to consist in nothing but proving to
himself every minute that he is a man and not a piano-key!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1408">
Text = It may be at
the cost of his skin, it may be by cannibalism!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1409">
Text = And this being so, can
one help being tempted to rejoice that it has not yet come off, and that
desire still depends on something we don't know?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1410">
Text = You will scream at me (that is, if you condescend to do so) that no one
is touching my free will, that all they are concerned with is that my
will should of itself, of its own free will, coincide with my own normal
interests, with the laws of nature and arithmetic.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1411">
Text = Good Heavens, gentlemen, what sort of free will is left when we come to
tabulation and arithmetic, when it will all be a case of twice two make
four?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1412">
Text = Twice two makes four without my will.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1413">
Text = As if free will meant that!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1414">
Text = IX
</Sent>
<Sent id="1415">
Text = Gentlemen, I am joking, and I know myself that my jokes are not
brilliant, but you know one can't take everything as a joke.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1416">
Text = I am,
perhaps, jesting against the grain.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1417">
Text = Gentlemen, I am tormented by
questions; answer them for me.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1418">
Text = You, for instance, want to cure men of
their old habits and reform their will in accordance with science and
good sense.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1419">
Text = But how do you know, not only that it is possible, but also
that it is _desirable_, to reform man in that way?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1420">
Text = And what leads you to
the conclusion that man's inclinations _need_ reforming?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1421">
Text = In short, how
do you know that such a reformation will be a benefit to man?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1422">
Text = And to go
to the root of the matter, why are you so positively convinced that not
to act against his real normal interests guaranteed by the conclusions
of reason and arithmetic is certainly always advantageous for man and
must always be a law for mankind?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1423">
Text = So far, you know, this is only your
supposition.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1424">
Text = It may be the law of logic, but not the law of humanity.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1425">
Text = You think, gentlemen, perhaps that I am mad?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1426">
Text = Allow me to defend myself.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1427">
Text = I agree that man is pre-eminently a creative animal, predestined to
strive consciously for an object and to engage in engineering--that is,
incessantly and eternally to make new roads, _wherever they may lead_.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1428">
Text = But the reason why he wants sometimes to go off at a tangent may just be
that he is _predestined_ to make the road, and perhaps, too, that
however stupid the "direct" practical man may be, the thought sometimes
will occur to him that the road almost always does lead _somewhere_, and
that the destination it leads to is less important than the process of
making it, and that the chief thing is to save the well-conducted child
from despising engineering, and so giving way to the fatal idleness,
which, as we all know, is the mother of all the vices.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1429">
Text = Man likes to make
roads and to create, that is a fact beyond dispute.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1430">
Text = But why has he such
a passionate love for destruction and chaos also?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1431">
Text = Tell me that!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1432">
Text = But on
that point I want to say a couple of words myself.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1433">
Text = May it not be that he
loves chaos and destruction (there can be no disputing that he does
sometimes love it) because he is instinctively afraid of attaining his
object and completing the edifice he is constructing?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1434">
Text = Who knows, perhaps
he only loves that edifice from a distance, and is by no means in love
with it at close quarters; perhaps he only loves building it and does
not want to live in it, but will leave it, when completed, for the use
of _les animaux domestiques_--such as the ants, the sheep, and so on.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1435">
Text = Now the ants have quite a different taste.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1436">
Text = They have a marvellous
edifice of that pattern which endures for ever--the ant-heap.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1437">
Text = With the ant-heap the respectable race of ants began and with the
ant-heap they will probably end, which does the greatest credit to their
perseverance and good sense.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1438">
Text = But man is a frivolous and incongruous
creature, and perhaps, like a chess player, loves the process of the
game, not the end of it.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1439">
Text = And who knows (there is no saying with
certainty), perhaps the only goal on earth to which mankind is striving
lies in this incessant process of attaining, in other words, in life
itself, and not in the thing to be attained, which must always be
expressed as a formula, as positive as twice two makes four, and such
positiveness is not life, gentlemen, but is the beginning of death.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1440">
Text = Anyway, man has always been afraid of this mathematical certainty, and I
am afraid of it now.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1441">
Text = Granted that man does nothing but seek that
mathematical certainty, he traverses oceans, sacrifices his life in the
quest, but to succeed, really to find it, he dreads, I assure you.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1442">
Text = He
feels that when he has found it there will be nothing for him to look
for.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1443">
Text = When workmen have finished their work they do at least receive
their pay, they go to the tavern, then they are taken to the
police-station--and there is occupation for a week.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1444">
Text = But where can man
go?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1445">
Text = Anyway, one can observe a certain awkwardness about him when he has
attained such objects.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1446">
Text = He loves the process of attaining, but does not
quite like to have attained, and that, of course, is very absurd.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1447">
Text = In
fact, man is a comical creature; there seems to be a kind of jest in it
all.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1448">
Text = But yet mathematical certainty is, after all, something
insufferable.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1449">
Text = Twice two makes four seems to me simply a piece of
insolence.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1450">
Text = Twice two makes four is a pert coxcomb who stands with arms
akimbo barring your path and spitting.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1451">
Text = I admit that twice two makes four
is an excellent thing, but if we are to give everything its due, twice
two makes five is sometimes a very charming thing too.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1452">
Text = And why are you so firmly, so triumphantly, convinced that only the
normal and the positive--in other words, only what is conducive to
welfare--is for the advantage of man?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1453">
Text = Is not reason in error as regards
advantage?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1454">
Text = Does not man, perhaps, love something besides well-being?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1455">
Text = Perhaps he is just as fond of suffering?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1456">
Text = Perhaps suffering is just as
great a benefit to him as well-being?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1457">
Text = Man is sometimes extraordinarily,
passionately, in love with suffering, and that is a fact.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1458">
Text = There is no
need to appeal to universal history to prove that; only ask yourself, if
you are a man and have lived at all.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1459">
Text = As far as my personal opinion is
concerned, to care only for well-being seems to me positively ill-bred.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1460">
Text = Whether it's good or bad, it is sometimes very pleasant, too, to smash
things.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1461">
Text = I hold no brief for suffering nor for well-being either.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1462">
Text = I am
standing for ... my caprice, and for its being guaranteed to me when
necessary.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1463">
Text = Suffering would be out of place in vaudevilles, for instance;
</Sent>
<Sent id="1464">
Text = I know that.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1465">
Text = In the "Palace of Crystal" it is unthinkable; suffering
means doubt, negation, and what would be the good of a "palace of
crystal" if there could be any doubt about it?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1466">
Text = And yet I think man will
never renounce real suffering, that is, destruction and chaos.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1467">
Text = Why,
suffering is the sole origin of consciousness.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1468">
Text = Though I did lay it down
at the beginning that consciousness is the greatest misfortune for man,
yet I know man prizes it and would not give it up for any satisfaction.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1469">
Text = Consciousness, for instance, is infinitely superior to twice two makes
four.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1470">
Text = Once you have mathematical certainty there is nothing left to do
or to understand.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1471">
Text = There will be nothing left but to bottle up your five
senses and plunge into contemplation.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1472">
Text = While if you stick to
consciousness, even though the same result is attained, you can at least
flog yourself at times, and that will, at any rate, liven you up.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1473">
Text = Reactionary as it is, corporal punishment is better than nothing.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1474">
Text = X
</Sent>
<Sent id="1475">
Text = You believe in a palace of crystal that can never be destroyed--a palace
at which one will not be able to put out one's tongue or make a long
nose on the sly.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1476">
Text = And perhaps that is just why I am afraid of this
edifice, that it is of crystal and can never be destroyed and that one
cannot put one's tongue out at it even on the sly.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1477">
Text = You see, if it were not a palace, but a hen-house, I might creep into it
to avoid getting wet, and yet I would not call the hen-house a palace
out of gratitude to it for keeping me dry.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1478">
Text = You laugh and say that in
such circumstances a hen-house is as good as a mansion.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1479">
Text = Yes, I answer,
if one had to live simply to keep out of the rain.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1480">
Text = But what is to be done if I have taken it into my head that that is not
the only object in life, and that if one must live one had better live
in a mansion.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1481">
Text = That is my choice, my desire.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1482">
Text = You will only eradicate it
when you have changed my preference.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1483">
Text = Well, do change it, allure me with
something else, give me another ideal.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1484">
Text = But meanwhile I will not take a
hen-house for a mansion.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1485">
Text = The palace of crystal may be an idle dream, it
may be that it is inconsistent with the laws of nature and that I have
invented it only through my own stupidity, through the old-fashioned
irrational habits of my generation.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1486">
Text = But what does it matter to me that
it is inconsistent?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1487">
Text = That makes no difference since it exists in my
desires, or rather exists as long as my desires exist.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1488">
Text = Perhaps you are
laughing again?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1489">
Text = Laugh away;
</Sent>
<Sent id="1490">
Text = I will put up with any mockery rather than
pretend that I am satisfied when I am hungry.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1491">
Text = I know, anyway, that I
will not be put off with a compromise, with a recurring zero, simply
because it is consistent with the laws of nature and actually exists.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1492">
Text = I
will not accept as the crown of my desires a block of buildings with
tenements for the poor on a lease of a thousand years, and perhaps with
a sign-board of a dentist hanging out.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1493">
Text = Destroy my desires, eradicate my
ideals, show me something better, and I will follow you.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1494">
Text = You will say,
perhaps, that it is not worth your trouble; but in that case I can give
you the same answer.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1495">
Text = We are discussing things seriously; but if you
won't deign to give me your attention, I will drop your acquaintance.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1496">
Text = I
can retreat into my underground hole.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1497">
Text = But while I am alive and have desires I would rather my hand were
withered off than bring one brick to such a building!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1498">
Text = Don't remind me
that I have just rejected the palace of crystal for the sole reason that
one cannot put out one's tongue at it.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1499">
Text = I did not say because I am so
fond of putting my tongue out.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1500">
Text = Perhaps the thing I resented was, that of
all your edifices there has not been one at which one could not put out
one's tongue.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1501">
Text = On the contrary, I would let my tongue be cut off out of
gratitude if things could be so arranged that I should lose all desire
to put it out.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1502">
Text = It is not my fault that things cannot be so arranged, and
that one must be satisfied with model flats.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1503">
Text = Then why am I made with
such desires?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1504">
Text = Can I have been constructed simply in order to come to the
conclusion that all my construction is a cheat?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1505">
Text = Can this be my whole
purpose?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1506">
Text = I do not believe it.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1507">
Text = But do you know what: I am convinced that we underground folk ought to
be kept on a curb.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1508">
Text = Though we may sit forty years underground without
speaking, when we do come out into the light of day and break out we
talk and talk and talk....
</Sent>
<Sent id="1509">
Text = XI
</Sent>
<Sent id="1510">
Text = The long and the short of it is, gentlemen, that it is better to do
nothing!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1511">
Text = Better conscious inertia!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1512">
Text = And so hurrah for underground!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1513">
Text = Though
I have said that I envy the normal man to the last drop of my bile, yet
I should not care to be in his place such as he is now (though I shall
not cease envying him).
</Sent>
<Sent id="1514">
Text = No, no; anyway the underground life is more
advantageous.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1515">
Text = There, at any rate, one can....
</Sent>
<Sent id="1516">
Text = Oh, but even now I am
lying!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1517">
Text = I am lying because I know myself that it is not underground that
is better, but something different, quite different, for which I am
thirsting, but which I cannot find!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1518">
Text = Damn underground!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1519">
Text = I will tell you another thing that would be better, and that is, if I
myself believed in anything of what I have just written.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1520">
Text = I swear to you,
gentlemen, there is not one thing, not one word of what I have written
that I really believe.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1521">
Text = That is, I believe it, perhaps, but at the same
time I feel and suspect that I am lying like a cobbler.

"Then why have you written all this?" you will say to me.

"I ought to put you underground for forty years without anything to do
and then come to you in your cellar, to find out what stage you have
reached!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1522">
Text = How can a man be left with nothing to do for forty years?"

"Isn't that shameful, isn't that humiliating?" you will say, perhaps,
wagging your heads contemptuously. "You thirst for life and try to
settle the problems of life by a logical tangle.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1523">
Text = And how persistent, how
insolent are your sallies, and at the same time what a scare you are in!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1524">
Text = You talk nonsense and are pleased with it; you say impudent things and
are in continual alarm and apologizing for them.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1525">
Text = You declare that you
are afraid of nothing and at the same time try to ingratiate yourself in
our good opinion.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1526">
Text = You declare that you are gnashing your teeth and at
the same time you try to be witty so as to amuse us.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1527">
Text = You know that your
witticisms are not witty, but you are evidently well satisfied with
their literary value.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1528">
Text = You may, perhaps, have really suffered, but you
have no respect for your own suffering.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1529">
Text = You may have sincerity, but you
have no modesty; out of the pettiest vanity you expose your sincerity to
publicity and ignominy.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1530">
Text = You doubtlessly mean to say something, but hide
your last word through fear, because you have not the resolution to
utter it, and only have a cowardly impudence.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1531">
Text = You boast of
consciousness, but you are not sure of your ground, for though your mind
works, yet your heart is darkened and corrupt, and you cannot have a
full, genuine consciousness without a pure heart.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1532">
Text = And how intrusive you
are, how you insist and grimace!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1533">
Text = Lies, lies, lies!"
</Sent>
<Sent id="1534">
Text = Of course I have myself made up all the things you say.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1535">
Text = That, too, is
from underground.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1536">
Text = I have been for forty years listening to you through a
crack under the floor.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1537">
Text = I have invented them myself, there was nothing
else I could invent.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1538">
Text = It is no wonder that I have learned it by heart and
it has taken a literary form....
</Sent>
<Sent id="1539">
Text = But can you really be so credulous as to think that I will print all
this and give it to you to read too?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1540">
Text = And another problem: why do I call
you "gentlemen," why do I address you as though you really were my
readers?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1541">
Text = Such confessions as I intend to make are never printed nor
given to other people to read.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1542">
Text = Anyway, I am not strong-minded enough for
that, and I don't see why I should be.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1543">
Text = But you see a fancy has occurred
to me and I want to realize it at all costs.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1544">
Text = Let me explain.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1545">
Text = Every man has reminiscences which he would not tell to every one, but
only to his friends.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1546">
Text = He has other matters in his mind which he would not
reveal even to his friends, but only to himself, and that in secret.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1547">
Text = But
there are other things which a man is afraid to tell even to himself,
and every decent man has a number of such things stored away in his
mind.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1548">
Text = The more decent he is, the greater the number of such things in
his mind.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1549">
Text = Anyway, I have only lately determined to remember some of my
early adventures.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1550">
Text = Till now I have always avoided them, even with a
certain uneasiness.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1551">
Text = Now, when I am not only recalling them, but have
actually decided to write an account of them, I want to try the
experiment whether one can, even with oneself, be perfectly open and not
take fright at the whole truth.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1552">
Text = I will observe, in parenthesis, that
Heine says that a true autobiography is almost an impossibility, and
that man is bound to lie about himself.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1553">
Text = He considers that Rousseau
certainly told lies about himself in his confessions, and even
intentionally lied, out of vanity.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1554">
Text = I am convinced that Heine is right;
</Sent>
<Sent id="1555">
Text = I
quite understand how sometimes one may, out of sheer vanity, attribute
regular crimes to oneself, and indeed I can very well conceive that kind
of vanity.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1556">
Text = But Heine judged of people who made their confessions to the
public.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1557">
Text = I write only for myself, and I wish to declare once and for all
that if I write as though I were addressing readers, that is simply
because it is easier for me to write in that form.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1558">
Text = It is a form, an
empty form--I shall never have readers.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1559">
Text = I have made this plain
already....
</Sent>
<Sent id="1560">
Text = I don't wish to be hampered by any restrictions in the compilation of my
notes.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1561">
Text = I shall not attempt any system or method.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1562">
Text = I will jot things down
as I remember them.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1563">
Text = But here, perhaps, some one will catch at the word and ask me: if you
really don't reckon on readers, why do you make such compacts with
yourself--and on paper too--that is, that you won't attempt any system
or method, that you jot things down as you remember them, and so on, and
so on?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1564">
Text = Why are you explaining?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1565">
Text = Why do you apologize?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1566">
Text = Well, there it is, I answer.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1567">
Text = There is a whole psychology in all this, though.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1568">
Text = Perhaps it is simply
that I am a coward.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1569">
Text = And perhaps that I purposely imagine an audience
before me in order that I may be more dignified while I write.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1570">
Text = There are
perhaps thousands of reasons.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1571">
Text = Again, what is my object precisely in
writing?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1572">
Text = If it is not for the benefit of the public why should I not
simply recall these incidents in my own mind without putting them on
paper?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1573">
Text = Quite so; but yet it is more imposing on paper.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1574">
Text = There is something more
impressive in it;
</Sent>
<Sent id="1575">
Text = I shall be better able to criticize myself and improve
my style.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1576">
Text = Besides, I shall perhaps obtain actual relief from writing.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1577">
Text = To-day, for instance, I am particularly oppressed by one memory of a
distant past.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1578">
Text = It came back vividly to my mind a few days ago, and has
remained haunting me like an annoying tune that one cannot get rid of.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1579">
Text = And yet I must get rid of it somehow.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1580">
Text = I have hundreds of such
reminiscences; but at times some one stands out from the hundred and
oppresses me.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1581">
Text = For some reason I believe that if I write it down I should
get rid of it.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1582">
Text = Why not try?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1583">
Text = Besides, I am bored, and I never have anything to do.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1584">
Text = Writing will be a
sort of work.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1585">
Text = They say work makes man kind-hearted and honest.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1586">
Text = Well,
here is a chance for me, anyway.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1587">
Text = Snow is falling to-day, yellow and dingy.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1588">
Text = It fell yesterday, too, and a
few days ago.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1589">
Text = I fancy it is the wet snow that has reminded me of that
incident which I cannot shake off now.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1590">
Text = And so let it be a story _à
propos_ of the falling snow.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1591">
Text = PART II

  À PROPOS OF THE WET SNOW
</Sent>
<Sent id="1592">
Text = When from dark error's subjugation
</Sent>
<Sent id="1593">
Text = My words of passionate exhortation
</Sent>
<Sent id="1594">
Text = Had wrenched thy fainting spirit free;
</Sent>
<Sent id="1595">
Text = And writhing prone in thine affliction
</Sent>
<Sent id="1596">
Text = Thou didst recall with malediction
</Sent>
<Sent id="1597">
Text = The vice that had encompassed thee:
</Sent>
<Sent id="1598">
Text = And when thy slumbering conscience, fretting
</Sent>
<Sent id="1599">
Text = By recollection's torturing flame,
</Sent>
<Sent id="1600">
Text = Thou didst reveal the hideous setting
</Sent>
<Sent id="1601">
Text = Of thy life's current ere I came:
</Sent>
<Sent id="1602">
Text = When suddenly I saw thee sicken,
</Sent>
<Sent id="1603">
Text = And weeping, hide thine anguished face,
</Sent>
<Sent id="1604">
Text = Revolted, maddened, horror-stricken,
</Sent>
<Sent id="1605">
Text = At memories of foul disgrace.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1606">
Text = NEKRASSOV (_translated by Juliet Soskice_).
</Sent>
<Sent id="1607">
Text = I
</Sent>
<Sent id="1608">
Text = At that time I was only twenty-four.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1609">
Text = My life was even then gloomy,
ill-regulated, and as solitary as that of a savage.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1610">
Text = I made friends with
no one and positively avoided talking, and buried myself more and more
in my hole.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1611">
Text = At work in the office I never looked at any one, and I was
perfectly well aware that my companions looked upon me, not only as a
queer fellow, but even looked upon me--I always fancied this--with a
sort of loathing.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1612">
Text = I sometimes wondered why it was that nobody except me
fancied that he was looked upon with aversion?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1613">
Text = One of the clerks had a
most repulsive, pock-marked face, which looked positively villainous.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1614">
Text = I
believe I should not have dared to look at any one with such an
unsightly countenance.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1615">
Text = Another had such a very dirty old uniform that
there was an unpleasant odour in his proximity.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1616">
Text = Yet not one of these
gentlemen showed the slightest self-consciousness--either about their
clothes or their countenance or their character in any way.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1617">
Text = Neither of
them ever imagined that they were looked at with repulsion; if they had
imagined it they would not have minded--so long as their superiors did
not look at them in that way.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1618">
Text = It is clear to me now that, owing to my
unbounded vanity and to the high standard I set for myself, I often
looked at myself with furious discontent, which verged on loathing, and
so I inwardly attributed the same feeling to every one.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1619">
Text = I hated my face,
for instance: I thought it disgusting, and even suspected that there was
something base in my expression, and so every day when I turned up at
the office I tried to behave as independently as possible, and to assume
a lofty expression, so that I might not be suspected of being abject.
"My face may be ugly," I thought, "but let it be lofty, expressive, and,
above all, _extremely_ intelligent." But I was positively and painfully
certain that it was impossible for my countenance ever to express those
qualities.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1620">
Text = And what was worst of all, I thought it actually stupid
looking, and I would have been quite satisfied if I could have looked
intelligent.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1621">
Text = In fact, I would even have put up with looking base if, at
the same time, my face could have been thought strikingly intelligent.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1622">
Text = Of course, I hated my fellow clerks one and all, and I despised them
all, yet at the same time I was, as it were, afraid of them.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1623">
Text = In fact, it
happened at times that I thought more highly of them than of myself.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1624">
Text = It
somehow happened quite suddenly that I alternated between despising them
and thinking them superior to myself.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1625">
Text = A cultivated and decent man cannot
be vain without setting a fearfully high standard for himself, and
without despising and almost hating himself at certain moments.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1626">
Text = But
whether I despised them or thought them superior I dropped my eyes
almost every time I met any one.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1627">
Text = I even made experiments whether I could
face so and so's looking at me, and I was always the first to drop my
eyes.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1628">
Text = This worried me to distraction.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1629">
Text = I had a sickly dread, too, of
being ridiculous, and so had a slavish passion for the conventional in
everything external.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1630">
Text = I loved to fall into the common rut, and had a
whole-hearted terror of any kind of eccentricity in myself.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1631">
Text = But how
could I live up to it?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1632">
Text = I was morbidly sensitive, as a man of our age
should be.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1633">
Text = They were all stupid, and as like one another as so many
sheep.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1634">
Text = Perhaps I was the only one in the office who fancied that I was a
coward and a slave, and I fancied it just because I was more highly
developed.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1635">
Text = But it was not only that I fancied it, it really was so.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1636">
Text = I
was a coward and a slave.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1637">
Text = I say this without the slightest
embarrassment.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1638">
Text = Every decent man of our age must be a coward and a slave.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1639">
Text = That is his normal condition.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1640">
Text = Of that I am firmly persuaded.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1641">
Text = He is made
and constructed to that very end.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1642">
Text = And not only at the present time owing
to some casual circumstances, but always, at all times, a decent man is
bound to be a coward and a slave.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1643">
Text = It is the law of nature for all decent
people all over the earth.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1644">
Text = If any one of them happens to be valiant
about something, he need not be comforted nor carried away by that; he
would show the white feather just the same before something else.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1645">
Text = That
is how it invariably and inevitably ends.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1646">
Text = Only donkeys and mules are
valiant, and they only till they are pushed up to the wall.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1647">
Text = It is not
worth while to pay attention to them for they really are of no
consequence.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1648">
Text = Another circumstance, too, worried me in those days: that there was no
one like me and I was unlike any one else. "I am alone and they are
_every one_," I thought--and pondered.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1649">
Text = From that it is evident that I was still a youngster.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1650">
Text = The very opposite sometimes happened.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1651">
Text = It was loathsome sometimes to go
to the office; things reached such a point that I often came home ill.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1652">
Text = But all at once, _à propos_ of nothing, there would come a phase of
scepticism and indifference (everything happened in phases to me), and I
would laugh myself at my intolerance and fastidiousness, I would
reproach myself with being _romantic_.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1653">
Text = At one time I was unwilling to
speak to any one, while at other times I would not only talk, but go to
the length of contemplating making friends with them.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1654">
Text = All my
fastidiousness would suddenly, for no rhyme or reason, vanish.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1655">
Text = Who
knows, perhaps I never had really had it, and it had simply been
affected, and got out of books.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1656">
Text = I have not decided that question even
now.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1657">
Text = Once I quite made friends with them, visited their homes, played
preference, drank vodka, talked of promotions....
</Sent>
<Sent id="1658">
Text = But here let me make a
digression.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1659">
Text = We Russians, speaking generally, have never had those foolish
transcendental "romantics"--German, and still more French--on whom
nothing produces any effect; if there were an earthquake, if all France
perished at the barricades, they would still be the same, they would not
even have the decency to affect a change, but would still go on singing
their transcendental songs to the hour of their death, because they are
fools.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1660">
Text = We, in Russia, have no fools; that is well known.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1661">
Text = That is what
distinguishes us from foreign lands.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1662">
Text = Consequently these transcendental
natures are not found amongst us in their pure form.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1663">
Text = The idea that they
are is due to our "realistic" journalists and critics of that day,
always on the look out for Kostanzhoglos and Uncle Pyotr Ivanitchs and
foolishly accepting them as our ideal; they have slandered our
romantics, taking them for the same transcendental sort as in Germany or
France.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1664">
Text = On the contrary, the characteristics of our "romantics" are
absolutely and directly opposed to the transcendental European type, and
no European standard can be applied to them. (Allow me to make use of
this word "romantic"--an old-fashioned and much respected word which has
done good service and is familiar to all).
</Sent>
<Sent id="1665">
Text = The characteristics of our
romantic are to understand everything, _to see everything and to see it
often incomparably more clearly than our most realistic minds see it_;
to refuse to accept anyone or anything, but at the same time not to
despise anything; to give way, to yield, from policy; never to lose
sight of a useful practical object (such as rent-free quarters at the
government expense, pensions, decorations), to keep their eye on that
object through all the enthusiasms and volumes of lyrical poems, and at
the same time to preserve "the good and the beautiful" inviolate within
them to the hour of their death, and to preserve themselves also,
incidentally, like some precious jewel wrapped in cotton wool if only
for the benefit of "the good and the beautiful." Our "romantic" is a man
of great breadth and the greatest rogue of all our rogues, I assure
you....
</Sent>
<Sent id="1666">
Text = I can assure you from experience, indeed.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1667">
Text = Of course, that is, if
he is intelligent.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1668">
Text = But what am I saying!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1669">
Text = The romantic is always
intelligent, and I only meant to observe that although we have had
foolish romantics they don't count, and they were only so because in the
flower of their youth they degenerated into Germans, and to preserve
their precious jewel more comfortably, settled somewhere out there--by
preference in Weimar or the Black Forest.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1670">
Text = I, for instance, genuinely despised my official work and did not openly
abuse it simply because I was in it myself and got a salary for it.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1671">
Text = Anyway, take note, I did not openly abuse it.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1672">
Text = Our romantic would rather
go out of his mind--a thing, however, which very rarely happens--than
take to open abuse, unless he had some other career in view; and he is
never kicked out.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1673">
Text = At most, they would take him to the lunatic asylum as
"the King of Spain" if he should go very mad.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1674">
Text = But it is only the thin,
fair people who go out of their minds in Russia.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1675">
Text = Innumerable "romantics"
attain later in life to considerable rank in the service.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1676">
Text = Their
many-sidedness is remarkable!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1677">
Text = And what a faculty they have for the most
contradictory sensations!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1678">
Text = I was comforted by this thought even in those
days, and I am of the same opinion now.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1679">
Text = That is why there are so many
"broad natures" among us who never lose their ideal even in the depths
of degradation; and though they never stir a finger for their ideal,
though they are arrant thieves and knaves, yet they tearfully cherish
their first ideal and are extraordinarily honest at heart.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1680">
Text = Yes, it is
only among us that the most incorrigible rogue can be absolutely and
loftily honest at heart without in the least ceasing to be a rogue.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1681">
Text = I
repeat, our romantics, frequently, become such accomplished rascals (I
use the term "rascals" affectionately), suddenly display such a sense of
reality and practical knowledge that their bewildered superiors and the
public generally can only ejaculate in amazement.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1682">
Text = Their many-sidedness is really amazing, and goodness knows what it may
develop into later on, and what the future has in store for us.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1683">
Text = It is
not a poor material!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1684">
Text = I do not say this from any foolish or boastful
patriotism.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1685">
Text = But I feel sure that you are again imagining that I am
joking.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1686">
Text = Or perhaps it's just the contrary, and you are convinced that I
really think so.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1687">
Text = Anyway, gentlemen, I shall welcome both views as an
honour and a special favour.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1688">
Text = And do forgive my digression.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1689">
Text = I did not, of course, maintain friendly relations with my comrades and
soon was at loggerheads with them, and in my youth and inexperience I
even gave up bowing to them, as though I had cut off all relations.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1690">
Text = That, however, only happened to me once.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1691">
Text = As a rule, I was always alone.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1692">
Text = In the first place I spent most of my time at home, reading.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1693">
Text = I tried to
stifle all that was continually seething within me by means of external
impressions.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1694">
Text = And the only external means I had was reading.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1695">
Text = Reading, of
course, was a great help--exciting me, giving me pleasure and pain.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1696">
Text = But
at times it bored me fearfully.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1697">
Text = One longed for movement in spite of
everything, and I plunged all at once into dark, underground, loathsome
vice of the pettiest kind.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1698">
Text = My wretched passions were acute, smarting,
from my continual, sickly irritability.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1699">
Text = I had hysterical impulses, with
tears and convulsions.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1700">
Text = I had no resource except reading, that is, there
was nothing in my surroundings which I could respect and which attracted
me.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1701">
Text = I was overwhelmed with depression, too;
</Sent>
<Sent id="1702">
Text = I had an hysterical craving
for incongruity and for contrast, and so I took to vice.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1703">
Text = I have not said
all this to justify myself....
</Sent>
<Sent id="1704">
Text = But, no!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1705">
Text = I am lying.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1706">
Text = I did want to
justify myself.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1707">
Text = I make that little observation for my own benefit,
gentlemen.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1708">
Text = I don't want to lie.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1709">
Text = I vowed to myself I would not.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1710">
Text = And so, furtively, timidly, in solitude, at night, I indulged in filthy
vice, with a feeling of shame which never deserted me, even at the most
loathsome moments, and which at such moments nearly made me curse.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1711">
Text = Already even then I had my underground world in my soul.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1712">
Text = I was fearfully
afraid of being seen, of being met, of being recognized.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1713">
Text = I visited
various obscure haunts.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1714">
Text = One night as I was passing a tavern I saw through a lighted window some
gentlemen fighting with billiard cues, and saw one of them thrown out of
window.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1715">
Text = At other times I should have felt very much disgusted, but I was
in such a mood at the time, that I actually envied the gentleman thrown
out of window--and I envied him so much that I even went into the tavern
and into the billiard-room. "Perhaps," I thought, "I'll have a fight,
too, and they'll throw me out of window."
</Sent>
<Sent id="1716">
Text = I was not drunk--but what is one to do--depression will drive a man to
such a pitch of hysteria?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1717">
Text = But nothing happened.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1718">
Text = It seemed that I was not
even equal to being thrown out of window and I went away without having
my fight.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1719">
Text = An officer put me in my place from the first moment.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1720">
Text = I was standing by the billiard-table and in my ignorance blocking up the
way, and he wanted to pass; he took me by the shoulders and without a
word--without a warning or explanation--moved me from where I was
standing to another spot and passed by as though he had not noticed me.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1721">
Text = I could have forgiven blows, but I could not forgive his having moved me
without noticing me.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1722">
Text = Devil knows what I would have given for a real regular quarrel--a more
decent, a more _literary_ one, so to speak.
</Sent>
