<Sent id="1723">
Text = I had been treated like a
fly.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1724">
Text = This officer was over six foot, while I was a spindly little
fellow.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1725">
Text = But the quarrel was in my hands.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1726">
Text = I had only to protest and I
certainly would have been thrown out of the window.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1727">
Text = But I changed my
mind and preferred to beat a resentful retreat.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1728">
Text = I went out of the tavern straight home, confused and troubled, and the
next night I went out again with the same lewd intentions, still more
furtively, abjectly and miserably than before, as it were, with tears in
my eyes--but still I did go out again.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1729">
Text = Don't imagine, though, it was
cowardice made me slink away from the officer: I never have been a
coward at heart, though I have always been a coward in action.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1730">
Text = Don't be
in a hurry to laugh--I assure you I can explain it all.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1731">
Text = Oh, if only that officer had been one of the sort who would consent to
fight a duel!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1732">
Text = But no, he was one of those gentlemen (alas, long
extinct!) who preferred fighting with cues or, like Gogol's Lieutenant
Pirogov, appealing to the police.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1733">
Text = They did not fight duels and would
have thought a duel with a civilian like me an utterly unseemly
procedure in any case--and they looked upon the duel altogether as
something impossible, something free-thinking and French.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1734">
Text = But they were
quite ready to bully, especially when they were over six foot.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1735">
Text = I did not slink away through cowardice, but through an unbounded vanity.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1736">
Text = I was afraid not of his six foot, not of getting a sound thrashing and
being thrown out of the window;
</Sent>
<Sent id="1737">
Text = I should have had physical courage
enough, I assure you; but I had not the moral courage.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1738">
Text = What I was afraid
of was that every one present, from the insolent marker down to the
lowest little stinking, pimply clerk in a greasy collar, would jeer at
me and fail to understand when I began to protest and to address them in
literary language.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1739">
Text = For of the point of honour--not of honour, but of the
point of honour (_point d'honneur_)--one cannot speak among us except in
literary language.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1740">
Text = You can't allude to the "point of honour" in ordinary
language.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1741">
Text = I was fully convinced (the sense of reality, in spite of all
my romanticism!) that they would all simply split their sides with
laughter, and that the officer would not simply beat me, that is,
without insulting me, but would certainly prod me in the back with his
knee, kick me round the billiard table, and only then perhaps have pity
and drop me out of the window.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1742">
Text = Of course, this trivial incident could not with me end in that.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1743">
Text = I often
met that officer afterwards in the street and noticed him very
carefully.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1744">
Text = I am not quite sure whether he recognized me, I imagine not;
</Sent>
<Sent id="1745">
Text = I judge from certain signs.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1746">
Text = But I--I stared at him with spite and hatred
and so it went on ... for several years!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1747">
Text = My resentment grew even deeper
with years.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1748">
Text = At first I began making stealthy inquiries about this
officer.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1749">
Text = It was difficult for me to do so, for I knew no one.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1750">
Text = But one
day I heard some one shout his surname in the street as I was following
him at a distance, as though I were tied to him--and so I learnt his
surname.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1751">
Text = Another time I followed him to his flat, and for ten kopecks
learned from the porter where he lived, on which storey, whether he
lived alone or with others, and so on--in fact, everything one could
learn from a porter.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1752">
Text = One morning, though I had never tried my hand with
the pen, it suddenly occurred to me to write a satire on this officer in
the form of a novel which would unmask his villainy.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1753">
Text = I wrote the novel
with relish.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1754">
Text = I did unmask his villainy, I even exaggerated it; at first
I so altered his surname that it could easily be recognized, but on
second thoughts I changed it, and sent the story to the
_Otetchestvenniya Zapiski_.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1755">
Text = But at that time such attacks were not the
fashion and my story was not printed.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1756">
Text = That was a great vexation to me.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1757">
Text = Sometimes I was positively choked with resentment.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1758">
Text = At last I determined
to challenge my enemy to a duel.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1759">
Text = I composed a splendid, charming letter
to him, imploring him to apologize to me, and hinting rather plainly at
a duel in case of refusal.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1760">
Text = The letter was so composed that if the
officer had had the least understanding of the good and the beautiful he
would certainly have flung himself on my neck and have offered me his
friendship.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1761">
Text = And how fine that would have been!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1762">
Text = How we should have got on
together! "He could have shielded me with his higher rank, while I could
have improved his mind with my culture, and, well ... my ideas, and all
sorts of things might have happened." Only fancy, this was two years
after his insult to me, and my challenge would have been a ridiculous
anachronism, in spite of all the ingenuity of my letter in disguising
and explaining away the anachronism.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1763">
Text = But, thank God (to this day I thank
the Almighty with tears in my eyes) I did not send the letter to him.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1764">
Text = Cold shivers run down my back when I think of what might have happened
if I had sent it.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1765">
Text = And all at once I revenged myself in the simplest way, by a stroke of
genius!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1766">
Text = A brilliant thought suddenly dawned upon me.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1767">
Text = Sometimes on
holidays I used to stroll along the sunny side of the Nevsky about four
o'clock in the afternoon.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1768">
Text = Though it was hardly a stroll so much as a
series of innumerable miseries, humiliations and resentments; but no
doubt that was just what I wanted.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1769">
Text = I used to wriggle along in a most
unseemly fashion, like an eel, continually moving aside to make way for
generals, for officers of the guards and the hussars, or for ladies.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1770">
Text = At
such minutes there used to be a convulsive twinge at my heart, and I
used to feel hot all down my back at the mere thought of the
wretchedness of my attire, of the wretchedness and abjectness of my
little scurrying figure.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1771">
Text = This was a regular martyrdom, a continual,
intolerable humiliation at the thought, which passed into an incessant
and direct sensation, that I was a mere fly in the eyes of all this
world, a nasty, disgusting fly--more intelligent, more highly developed,
more refined in feeling than any of them, of course--but a fly that was
continually making way for every one, insulted and injured by every one.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1772">
Text = Why I inflicted this torture upon myself, why I went to the Nevsky, I
don't know.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1773">
Text = I felt simply drawn there at every possible opportunity.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1774">
Text = Already then I began to experience a rush of the enjoyment of which I
spoke in the first chapter.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1775">
Text = After my affair with the officer I felt even
more drawn there than before: it was on the Nevsky that I met him most
frequently, there I could admire him.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1776">
Text = He, too, went there chiefly on
holidays.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1777">
Text = He, too, turned out of his path for generals and persons of
high rank, and he, too, wriggled between them like an eel; but people,
like me, or even better dressed like me, he simply walked over; he made
straight for them as though there was nothing but empty space before
him, and never, under any circumstances, turned aside.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1778">
Text = I gloated over my
resentment watching him and ... always resentfully made way for him.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1779">
Text = It
exasperated me that even in the street I could not be on an even footing
with him.

"Why must you invariably be the first to move aside?" I kept asking
myself in hysterical rage, waking up sometimes at three o'clock in the
morning. "Why is it you and not he?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1780">
Text = There's no regulation about it;
there's no written law.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1781">
Text = Let the making way be equal as it usually is
when refined people meet: he moves half-way and you move half-way; you
pass with mutual respect."
</Sent>
<Sent id="1782">
Text = But that never happened, and I always moved aside, while he did not even
notice my making way for him.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1783">
Text = And lo and behold a bright idea dawned
upon me! "What," I thought, "if I meet him and don't move on one side?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1784">
Text = What if I don't move aside on purpose, even if I knock up against him?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1785">
Text = How would that be?" This audacious idea took such a hold on me that it
gave me no peace.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1786">
Text = I was dreaming of it continually, horribly, and I
purposely went more frequently to the Nevsky in order to picture more
vividly how I should do it when I did do it.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1787">
Text = I was delighted.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1788">
Text = This
intention seemed to me more and more practical and possible.

"Of course I shall not really push him," I thought, already more
good-natured in my joy. "I will simply not turn aside, will run up
against him, not very violently, but just shouldering each other--just
as much as decency permits.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1789">
Text = I will push against him just as much as he
pushes against me." At last I made up my mind completely.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1790">
Text = But my
preparations took a great deal of time.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1791">
Text = To begin with, when I carried
out my plan I should need to be looking rather more decent, and so I had
to think of my get-up. "In case of emergency, if, for instance, there
were any sort of public scandal (and the public there is of the most
_recherché_: the Countess walks there;
</Sent>
<Sent id="1792">
Text = Prince D. walks there; all the
literary world is there), I must be well dressed; that inspires respect
and of itself puts us on an equal footing in the eyes of society."
</Sent>
<Sent id="1793">
Text = With this object I asked for some of my salary in advance, and bought at
Tchurkin's a pair of black gloves and a decent hat.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1794">
Text = Black gloves seemed
to me both more dignified and _bon ton_ than the lemon-coloured ones
which I had contemplated at first. "The colour is too gaudy, it looks as
though one were trying to be conspicuous," and I did not take the
lemon-coloured ones.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1795">
Text = I had got ready long beforehand a good shirt, with
white bone studs; my overcoat was the only thing that held me back.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1796">
Text = The
coat in itself was a very good one, it kept me warm; but it was wadded
and it had a raccoon collar which was the height of vulgarity.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1797">
Text = I had to
change the collar at any sacrifice, and to have a beaver one like an
officer's. For this purpose I began visiting the Gostiny Dvor and after
several attempts I pitched upon a piece of cheap German beaver.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1798">
Text = Though
these German beavers soon grow shabby and look wretched, yet at first
they look exceedingly well, and I only needed it for one occasion.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1799">
Text = I
asked the price; even so, it was too expensive.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1800">
Text = After thinking it over
thoroughly I decided to sell my raccoon collar.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1801">
Text = The rest of the money--a
considerable sum for me, I decided to borrow from Anton Antonitch
Syetotchkin, my immediate superior, an unassuming person, though grave
and judicious.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1802">
Text = He never lent money to any one, but I had, on entering
the service, been specially recommended to him by an important personage
who had got me my berth.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1803">
Text = I was horribly worried.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1804">
Text = To borrow from Anton
Antonitch seemed to me monstrous and shameful.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1805">
Text = I did not sleep for two
or three nights.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1806">
Text = Indeed, I did not sleep well at that time, I was in a
fever;
</Sent>
<Sent id="1807">
Text = I had a vague sinking at my heart or else a sudden throbbing,
throbbing, throbbing!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1808">
Text = Anton Antonitch was surprised at first, then he
frowned, then he reflected, and did after all lend me the money,
receiving from me a written authorization to take from my salary a
fortnight later the sum that he had lent me.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1809">
Text = In this way everything was at last ready.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1810">
Text = The handsome beaver replaced
the mean-looking raccoon, and I began by degrees to get to work.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1811">
Text = It
would never have done to act off-hand, at random; the plan had to be
carried out skilfully, by degrees.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1812">
Text = But I must confess that after many
efforts I began to despair: we simply could not run into each other.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1813">
Text = I
made every preparation, I was quite determined--it seemed as though we
should run into one another directly--and before I knew what I was doing
I had stepped aside for him again and he had passed without noticing me.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1814">
Text = I even prayed as I approached him that God would grant me determination.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1815">
Text = One time I had made up my mind thoroughly, but it ended in my stumbling
and falling at his feet because at the very last instant when I was six
inches from him my courage failed me.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1816">
Text = He very calmly stepped over me,
while I flew on one side like a ball.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1817">
Text = That night I was ill again,
feverish and delirious.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1818">
Text = And suddenly it ended most happily.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1819">
Text = The night before I had made up my
mind not to carry out my fatal plan and to abandon it all, and with that
object I went to the Nevsky for the last time, just to see how I would
abandon it all.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1820">
Text = Suddenly, three paces from my enemy, I unexpectedly made
up my mind--I closed my eyes, and we ran full tilt, shoulder to
shoulder, against one another!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1821">
Text = I did not budge an inch and passed him on
a perfectly equal footing!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1822">
Text = He did not even look round and pretended not
to notice it; but he was only pretending, I am convinced of that.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1823">
Text = I am
convinced of that to this day!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1824">
Text = Of course, I got the worst of it--he was
stronger, but that was not the point.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1825">
Text = The point was that I had attained
my object, I had kept up my dignity, I had not yielded a step, and had
put myself publicly on an equal social footing with him.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1826">
Text = I returned home
feeling that I was fully avenged for everything.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1827">
Text = I was delighted.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1828">
Text = I was
triumphant and sang Italian arias.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1829">
Text = Of course, I will not describe to you
what happened to me three days later; if you have read my first chapter
you can guess that for yourself.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1830">
Text = The officer was afterwards transferred;
</Sent>
<Sent id="1831">
Text = I have not seen him now for fourteen years.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1832">
Text = What is the dear fellow
doing now?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1833">
Text = Whom is he walking over?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1834">
Text = II
</Sent>
<Sent id="1835">
Text = But the period of my dissipation would end and I always felt very sick
afterwards.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1836">
Text = It was followed by remorse--I tried to drive it away: I felt
too sick.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1837">
Text = By degrees, however, I grew used to that too.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1838">
Text = I grew used to
everything, or rather I voluntarily resigned myself to enduring it.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1839">
Text = But
I had a means of escape that reconciled everything--that was to find
refuge in "the good and the beautiful," in dreams, of course.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1840">
Text = I was a
terrible dreamer, I would dream for three months on end, tucked away in
my corner, and you may believe me that at those moments I had no
resemblance to the gentleman who, in the perturbation of his chicken
heart, put a collar of German beaver on his great coat.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1841">
Text = I suddenly
became a hero.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1842">
Text = I would not have admitted my six-foot lieutenant even if
he had called on me.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1843">
Text = I could not even picture him before me then.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1844">
Text = What
were my dreams and how I could satisfy myself with them--it is hard to
say now, but at the time I was satisfied with them.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1845">
Text = Though, indeed, even
now, I am to some extent satisfied with them.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1846">
Text = Dreams were particularly
sweet and vivid after a spell of dissipation; they came with remorse and
with tears, with curses and transports.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1847">
Text = There were moments of such
positive intoxication, of such happiness, that there was not the
faintest trace of irony within me, on my honour.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1848">
Text = I had faith, hope,
love.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1849">
Text = I believed blindly at such times that by some miracle, by some
external circumstance, all this would suddenly open out, expand; that
suddenly a vista of suitable activity--beneficent, good, and, above all,
_ready made_ (what sort of activity I had no idea, but the great thing
was that it should be all ready for me)--would rise up before me--and I
should come out into the light of day, almost riding a white horse and
crowned with laurel.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1850">
Text = Anything but the foremost place I could not
conceive for myself, and for that very reason I quite contentedly
occupied the lowest in reality.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1851">
Text = Either to be a hero or to grovel in the
mud--there was nothing between.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1852">
Text = That was my ruin, for when I was in the
mud I comforted myself with the thought that at other times I was a
hero, and the hero was a cloak for the mud: for an ordinary man it was
shameful to defile himself, but a hero was too lofty to be utterly
defiled, and so he might defile himself.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1853">
Text = It is worth noting that these
attacks of the "good and the beautiful" visited me even during the
period of dissipation and just at the times when I was touching the
bottom.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1854">
Text = They came in separate spurts, as though reminding me of
themselves, but did not banish the dissipation by their appearance.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1855">
Text = On
the contrary, they seemed to add a zest to it by contrast, and were only
sufficiently present to serve as an appetizing sauce.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1856">
Text = That sauce was
made up of contradictions and sufferings, of agonizing inward analysis
and all these pangs and pin-pricks gave a certain piquancy, even a
significance to my dissipation--in fact, completely answered the purpose
of an appetizing sauce.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1857">
Text = There was a certain depth of meaning in it.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1858">
Text = And
I could hardly have resigned myself to the simple, vulgar, direct
debauchery of a clerk and have endured all the filthiness of it.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1859">
Text = What
could have allured me about it then and have drawn me at night into the
street?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1860">
Text = No, I had a lofty way of getting out of it all.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1861">
Text = And what loving-kindness, oh Lord, what loving-kindness I felt at times
in those dreams of mine! in those "flights into the good and the
beautiful;" though it was fantastic love, though it was never applied to
anything human in reality, yet there was so much of this love that one
did not feel afterwards even the impulse to apply it in reality; that
would have been superfluous.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1862">
Text = Everything, however, passed satisfactorily
by a lazy and fascinating transition into the sphere of art, that is,
into the beautiful forms of life, lying ready, largely stolen from the
poets and novelists and adapted to all sorts of needs and uses.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1863">
Text = I, for
instance, was triumphant over every one; every one, of course, was in
dust and ashes, and was forced spontaneously to recognize my
superiority, and I forgave them all.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1864">
Text = I was a poet and a grand gentleman,
I fell in love;
</Sent>
<Sent id="1865">
Text = I came in for countless millions and immediately devoted
them to humanity, and at the same time I confessed before all the people
my shameful deeds, which, of course, were not merely shameful, but had
in them much that was "good and beautiful," something in the Manfred
style.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1866">
Text = Every one would kiss me and weep (what idiots they would be if
they did not), while I should go barefoot and hungry preaching new ideas
and fighting a victorious Austerlitz against the obscurantists.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1867">
Text = Then the
band would play a march, an amnesty would be declared, the Pope would
agree to retire from Rome to Brazil; then there would be a ball for the
whole of Italy at the Villa Borghese on the shores of the Lake of Como,
the Lake of Como being for that purpose transferred to the neighbourhood
of Rome; then would come a scene in the bushes, and so on, and so on--as
though you did not know all about it?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1868">
Text = You will say that it is vulgar and
contemptible to drag all this into public after all the tears and
transports which I have myself confessed.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1869">
Text = But why is it contemptible?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1870">
Text = Can you imagine that I am ashamed of it all, and that it was stupider
than anything in your life, gentlemen?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1871">
Text = And I can assure you that some of
these fancies were by no means badly composed....
</Sent>
<Sent id="1872">
Text = It did not all happen
on the shores of Lake Como.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1873">
Text = And yet you are right--it really is vulgar
and contemptible.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1874">
Text = And most contemptible of all it is that now I am
attempting to justify myself to you.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1875">
Text = And even more contemptible than
that is my making this remark now.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1876">
Text = But that's enough, or there will be
no end to it: each step will be more contemptible than the last....
</Sent>
<Sent id="1877">
Text = I could never stand more than three months of dreaming at a time without
feeling an irresistible desire to plunge into society.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1878">
Text = To plunge into
society meant to visit my superior at the office, Anton Antonitch
Syetotchkin.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1879">
Text = He was the only permanent acquaintance I have had in my
life, and wonder at the fact myself now.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1880">
Text = But I only went to see him when
that phase came over me, and when my dreams had reached such a point of
bliss that it became essential at once to embrace my fellows and all
mankind; and for that purpose I needed, at least, one human being,
actually existing.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1881">
Text = I had to call on Anton Antonitch, however, on
Tuesday--his at-home day; so I had always to time my passionate desire
to embrace humanity so that it might fall on a Tuesday.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1882">
Text = This Anton Antonitch lived on the fourth storey in a house in Five
Corners, in four low-pitched rooms, one smaller than the other, of a
particularly frugal and sallow appearance.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1883">
Text = He had two daughters and
their aunt, who used to pour out the tea.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1884">
Text = Of the daughters one was
thirteen and another fourteen, they both had snub noses, and I was
awfully shy of them because they were always whispering and giggling
together.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1885">
Text = The master of the house usually sat in his study on a leather
couch in front of the table with some grey-headed gentleman, usually a
colleague from our office or some other department.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1886">
Text = I never saw more
than two or three visitors there, always the same.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1887">
Text = They talked about the
excise duty; about business in the senate, about salaries, about
promotions, about His Excellency, and the best means of pleasing him,
and so on.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1888">
Text = I had the patience to sit like a fool beside these people for
four hours at a stretch, listening to them without knowing what to say
to them or venturing to say a word.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1889">
Text = I became stupified, several times I
felt myself perspiring, I was overcome by a sort of paralysis; but this
was pleasant and good for me.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1890">
Text = On returning home I deferred for a time my
desire to embrace all mankind.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1891">
Text = I had however one other acquaintance of a sort, Simonov, who was an old
schoolfellow.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1892">
Text = I had a number of schoolfellows indeed in Petersburg, but
I did not associate with them and had even given up nodding to them in
the street.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1893">
Text = I believe I had transferred into the department I was in
simply to avoid their company and to cut off all connection with my
hateful childhood.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1894">
Text = Curses on that school and all those terrible years of
penal servitude!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1895">
Text = In short, I parted from my schoolfellows as soon as I
got out into the world.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1896">
Text = There were two or three left to whom I nodded in
the street.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1897">
Text = One of them was Simonov, who had been in no way
distinguished at school, was of a quiet and equable disposition; but I
discovered in him a certain independence of character and even honesty.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1898">
Text = I don't even suppose that he was particularly stupid.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1899">
Text = I had at one time
spent some rather soulful moments with him, but these had not lasted
long and had somehow been suddenly clouded over.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1900">
Text = He was evidently
uncomfortable at these reminiscences, and was, I fancy, always afraid
that I might take up the same tone again.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1901">
Text = I suspected that he had an
aversion for me, but still I went on going to see him, not being quite
certain of it.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1902">
Text = And so on one occasion, unable to endure my solitude and knowing that as
it was Thursday Anton Antonitch's door would be closed, I thought of
Simonov.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1903">
Text = Climbing up to his fourth storey I was thinking that the man
disliked me and that it was a mistake to go and see him.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1904">
Text = But as it
always happened that such reflections impelled me, as though purposely,
to put myself into a false position, I went in.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1905">
Text = It was almost a year
since I had last seen Simonov.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1906">
Text = III
</Sent>
<Sent id="1907">
Text = I found two of my old schoolfellows with him.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1908">
Text = They seemed to be
discussing an important matter.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1909">
Text = All of them took scarcely any notice of
my entrance, which was strange, for I had not met them for years.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1910">
Text = Evidently they looked upon me as something on the level of a common fly.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1911">
Text = I had not been treated like that even at school, though they all hated
me.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1912">
Text = I knew, of course, that they must despise me now for my lack of
success in the service, and for my having let myself sink so low, going
about badly dressed and so on--which seemed to them a sign of my
incapacity and insignificance.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1913">
Text = But I had not expected such contempt.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1914">
Text = Simonov was positively surprised at my turning up.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1915">
Text = Even in old days he
had always seemed surprised at my coming.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1916">
Text = All this disconcerted me: I
sat down, feeling rather miserable, and began listening to what they
were saying.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1917">
Text = They were engaged in warm and earnest conversation about a farewell
dinner which they wanted to arrange for the next day to a comrade of
theirs called Zverkov, an officer in the army, who was going away to a
distant province.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1918">
Text = This Zverkov had been all the time at school with me
too.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1919">
Text = I had begun to hate him particularly in the upper forms.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1920">
Text = In the
lower forms he had simply been a pretty, playful boy whom everybody
liked.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1921">
Text = I had hated him, however, even in the lower forms, just because
he was a pretty and playful boy.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1922">
Text = He was always bad at his lessons and
got worse and worse as he went on; however, he left with a good
certificate, as he had powerful interest.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1923">
Text = During his last year at school
he came in for an estate of two hundred serfs, and as almost all of us
were poor he took up a swaggering tone among us.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1924">
Text = He was vulgar in the
extreme, but at the same time he was a good-natured fellow, even in his
swaggering.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1925">
Text = In spite of superficial, fantastic and sham notions of
honour and dignity, all but very few of us positively grovelled before
Zverkov, and the more so the more he swaggered.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1926">
Text = And it was not from any
interested motive that they grovelled, but simply because he had been
favoured by the gifts of nature.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1927">
Text = Moreover, it was, as it were, an
accepted idea among us that Zverkov was a specialist in regard to tact
and the social graces.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1928">
Text = This last fact particularly infuriated me.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1929">
Text = I
hated the abrupt self-confident tone of his voice, his admiration of his
own witticisms, which were often frightfully stupid, though he was bold
in his language;
</Sent>
<Sent id="1930">
Text = I hated his handsome, but stupid face (for which I
would, however, have gladly exchanged my intelligent one), and the
free-and-easy military manners in fashion in the "'forties." I hated the
way in which he used to talk of his future conquests of women (he did
not venture to begin his attack upon women until he had the epaulettes
of an officer, and was looking forward to them with impatience), and
boasted of the duels he would constantly be fighting.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1931">
Text = I remember how I,
invariably so taciturn, suddenly fastened upon Zverkov, when one day
talking at a leisure moment with his schoolfellows of his future
relations with the fair sex, and growing as sportive as a puppy in the
sun, he all at once declared that he would not leave a single village
girl on his estate unnoticed, that that was his _droit de seigneur_, and
that if the peasants dared to protest he would have them all flogged and
double the tax on them, the bearded rascals.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1932">
Text = Our servile rabble
applauded, but I attacked him, not from compassion for the girls and
their fathers, but simply because they were applauding such an insect.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1933">
Text = I
got the better of him on that occasion, but though Zverkov was stupid he
was lively and impudent, and so laughed it off, and in such a way that
my victory was not really complete: the laugh was on his side.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1934">
Text = He got
the better of me on several occasions afterwards, but without malice,
jestingly, casually.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1935">
Text = I remained angrily and contemptuously silent and
would not answer him.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1936">
Text = When we left school he made advances to me;
</Sent>
<Sent id="1937">
Text = I did
not rebuff them, for I was flattered, but we soon parted and quite
naturally.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1938">
Text = Afterwards I heard of his barrack-room success as a
lieutenant, and of the fast life he was leading.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1939">
Text = Then there came other
rumours--of his successes in the service.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1940">
Text = By then he had taken to
cutting me in the street, and I suspected that he was afraid of
compromising himself by greeting a personage as insignificant as me.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1941">
Text = I
saw him once in the theatre, in the third tier of boxes.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1942">
Text = By then he was
wearing shoulder-straps.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1943">
Text = He was twisting and twirling about,
ingratiating himself with the daughters of an ancient General.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1944">
Text = In three
years he had gone off considerably, though he was still rather handsome
and adroit.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1945">
Text = One could see that by the time he was thirty he would be
corpulent.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1946">
Text = So it was to this Zverkov that my schoolfellows were going to
give a dinner on his departure.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1947">
Text = They had kept up with him for those
three years, though privately they did not consider themselves on an
equal footing with him, I am convinced of that.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1948">
Text = Of Simonov's two visitors, one was Ferfitchkin, a Russianized German--a
little fellow with the face of a monkey, a blockhead who was always
deriding every one, a very bitter enemy of mine from our days in the
lower forms--a vulgar, impudent, swaggering fellow, who affected a most
sensitive feeling of personal honour, though, of course, he was a
wretched little coward at heart.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1949">
Text = He was one of those worshippers of
Zverkov who made up to the latter from interested motives, and often
borrowed money from him.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1950">
Text = Simonov's other visitor, Trudolyubov, was a
person in no way remarkable--a tall young fellow, in the army, with a
cold face, fairly honest, though he worshipped success of every sort,
and was only capable of thinking of promotion.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1951">
Text = He was some sort of
distant relation of Zverkov's, and this, foolish as it seems, gave him a
certain importance among us.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1952">
Text = He always thought me of no consequence
whatever; his behaviour to me, though not quite courteous, was
tolerable.

"Well, with seven roubles each," said Trudolyubov, "twenty-one roubles
between the three of us, we ought to be able to get a good dinner.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1953">
Text = Zverkov, of course, won't pay."

"Of course not, since we are inviting him," Simonov decided.

"Can you imagine," Ferfitchkin interrupted hotly and conceitedly, like
some insolent flunkey boasting of his master the General's decorations,
"can you imagine that Zverkov will let us pay alone?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1954">
Text = He will accept from
delicacy, but he will order half a dozen bottles of champagne."

"Do we want half a dozen for the four of us?" observed Trudolyubov,
taking notice only of the half dozen.

"So the three of us, with Zverkov for the fourth, twenty-one roubles, at
the Hôtel de Paris at five o'clock to-morrow," Simonov, who had been
asked to make the arrangements, concluded finally.

"How twenty-one roubles?" I asked in some agitation, with a show of
being offended; "if you count me it will not be twenty-one, but
twenty-eight roubles."
</Sent>
<Sent id="1955">
Text = It seemed to me that to invite myself so suddenly and unexpectedly would
be positively graceful, and that they would all be conquered at once and
would look at me with respect.

"Do you want to join, too?" Simonov observed, with no appearance of
pleasure, seeming to avoid looking at me.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1956">
Text = He knew me through and
through.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1957">
Text = It infuriated me that he knew me so thoroughly.

"Why not?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1958">
Text = I am an old schoolfellow of his, too, I believe, and I must
own I feel hurt that you have left me out," I said, boiling over again.

"And where were we to find you?" Ferfitchkin put in roughly.

"You never were on good terms with Zverkov," Trudolyubov added,
frowning.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1959">
Text = But I had already clutched at the idea and would not give it up.

"It seems to me that no one has a right to form an opinion upon that," I
retorted in a shaking voice, as though something tremendous had
happened. "Perhaps that is just my reason for wishing it now, that I
have not always been on good terms with him."

"Oh, there's no making you out ... with these refinements," Trudolyubov
jeered.

"We'll put your name down," Simonov decided, addressing me. "To-morrow
at five o'clock at the Hôtel de Paris."

"What about the money?" Ferfitchkin began in an undertone, indicating me
to Simonov, but he broke off, for even Simonov was embarrassed.

"That will do," said Trudolyubov, getting up. "If he wants to come so
much, let him."

"But it's a private thing, between us friends," Ferfitchkin said
crossly, as he, too, picked up his hat. "It's not an official
gathering."

"We do not want at all, perhaps...."
</Sent>
<Sent id="1960">
Text = They went away.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1961">
Text = Ferfitchkin did not greet me in any way as he went out,
Trudolyubov barely nodded.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1962">
Text = Simonov, with whom I was left _tête-à-tête_,
was in a state of vexation and perplexity, and looked at me queerly.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1963">
Text = He
did not sit down and did not ask me to.

"H'm ... yes ... to-morrow, then.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1964">
Text = Will you pay your subscription now?
</Sent>
<Sent id="1965">
Text = I
just ask so as to know," he muttered in embarrassment.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1966">
Text = I flushed crimson, and as I did so I remembered that I had owed Simonov
fifteen roubles for ages--which I had, indeed, never forgotten, though I
had not paid it.

"You will understand, Simonov, that I could have no idea when I came
here....
</Sent>
<Sent id="1967">
Text = I am very much vexed that I have forgotten...."

"All right, all right, that doesn't matter.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1968">
Text = You can pay to-morrow after
the dinner.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1969">
Text = I simply wanted to know....
</Sent>
<Sent id="1970">
Text = Please don't...."
</Sent>
<Sent id="1971">
Text = He broke off and began pacing the room still more vexed.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1972">
Text = As he walked he
began to stamp with his heels.

"Am I keeping you?" I asked, after two minutes of silence.

"Oh!" he said, starting, "that is--to be truthful--yes.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1973">
Text = I have to go and
see some one ... not far from here," he added in an apologetic voice,

"My goodness, why didn't you say so?" I cried, seizing my cap, with an

"It's close by ... not two paces away," Simonov repeated, accompanying

</Sent>
<Sent id="1974">
Text = He was very glad to get rid of me.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1975">
Text = I was in a fury.

"What possessed me, what possessed me to force myself upon them?" I
wondered, grinding my teeth as I strode along the street, "for a

</Sent>
<Sent id="1976">
Text = Of course, I had better not go; of
course, I must just snap my fingers at them.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1977">
Text = I am not bound in any way.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1978">
Text = I'll send Simonov a note by to-morrow's post...."
</Sent>
<Sent id="1979">
Text = But what made me furious was that I knew for certain that I should go,
that I should make a point of going; and the more tactless, the more

</Sent>
<Sent id="1980">
Text = And there was a positive obstacle to my going: I had no money.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1981">
Text = All I had
was nine roubles, I had to give seven of that to my servant, Apollon,
for his monthly wages.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1982">
Text = That was all I paid him--he had to keep himself.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1983">
Text = Not to pay him was impossible, considering his character.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1984">
Text = But I will
talk about that fellow, about that plague of mine, another time.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1985">
Text = However, I knew I should go and should not pay him his wages.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1986">
Text = That night I had the most hideous dreams.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1987">
Text = No wonder; all the evening I
had been oppressed by memories of my miserable days at school, and I
could not shake them off.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1988">
Text = I was sent to the school by distant relations,
upon whom I was dependent and of whom I have heard nothing since--they
sent me there a forlorn, silent boy, already crushed by their
reproaches, already troubled by doubt, and looking with savage distrust
at every one.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1989">
Text = My schoolfellows met me with spiteful and merciless jibes
because I was not like any of them.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1990">
Text = But I could not endure their taunts;
</Sent>
<Sent id="1991">
Text = I could not give in to them with the ignoble readiness with which they
gave in to one another.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1992">
Text = I hated them from the first, and shut myself
away from every one in timid, wounded and disproportionate pride.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1993">
Text = Their
coarseness revolted me.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1994">
Text = They laughed cynically at my face, at my clumsy
figure; and yet what stupid faces they had themselves.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1995">
Text = In our school the
boys' faces seemed in a special way to degenerate and grow stupider.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1996">
Text = How
many fine-looking boys came to us!
</Sent>
<Sent id="1997">
Text = In a few years they became repulsive.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1998">
Text = Even at sixteen I wondered at them morosely; even then I was struck by
the pettiness of their thoughts, the stupidity of their pursuits, their
games, their conversations.
</Sent>
<Sent id="1999">
Text = They had no understanding of such essential
things, they took no interest in such striking, impressive subjects,
that I could not help considering them inferior to myself.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2000">
Text = It was not
wounded vanity that drove me to it, and for God's sake do not thrust
upon me your hackneyed remarks, repeated to nausea, that "I was only a
dreamer," while they even then had an understanding of life.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2001">
Text = They
understood nothing, they had no idea of real life, and I swear that that
was what made me most indignant with them.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2002">
Text = On the contrary, the most
obvious, striking reality they accepted with fantastic stupidity and
even at that time were accustomed to respect success.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2003">
Text = Everything that
was just, but oppressed and looked down upon, they laughed at
heartlessly and shamefully.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2004">
Text = They took rank for intelligence; even at
sixteen they were already talking about a snug berth.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2005">
Text = Of course, a great
deal of it was due to their stupidity, to the bad examples with which
they had always been surrounded in their childhood and boyhood.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2006">
Text = They
were monstrously depraved.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2007">
Text = Of course a great deal of that, too, was
superficial and an assumption of cynicism; of course there were glimpses
of youth and freshness even in their depravity; but even that freshness
was not attractive, and showed itself in a certain rakishness.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2008">
Text = I hated
them horribly, though perhaps I was worse than any of them.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2009">
Text = They repaid
me in the same way, and did not conceal their aversion for me.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2010">
Text = But by
then I did not desire their affection: on the contrary I continually
longed for their humiliation.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2011">
Text = To escape from their derision I purposely
began to make all the progress I could with my studies and forced my way
to the very top.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2012">
Text = This impressed them.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2013">
Text = Moreover, they all began by
degrees to grasp that I had already read books none of them could read,
and understood things (not forming part of our school curriculum) of
which they had not even heard.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2014">
Text = They took a savage and sarcastic view of
it, but were morally impressed, especially as the teachers began to
notice me on those grounds.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2015">
Text = The mockery ceased, but the hostility
remained, and cold and strained relations became permanent between us.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2016">
Text = In the end I could not put up with it: with years a craving for society,
for friends, developed in me.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2017">
Text = I attempted to get on friendly terms with
some of my schoolfellows; but somehow or other my intimacy with them was
always strained and soon ended of itself.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2018">
Text = Once, indeed, I did have a
friend.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2019">
Text = But I was already a tyrant at heart;
</Sent>

</Sent>

</Sent>

</Sent>

</Sent>

</Sent>

</Sent>
<Sent id="2026">
Text = But I could not

</Sent>
<Sent id="2027">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2028">

</Sent>

</Sent>
<Sent id="2030">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2031">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2032">
Text = I went to the office, however, as usual,

</Sent>
<Sent id="2033">
Text = The great thing, I

</Sent>
<Sent id="2034">
Text = But there were thousands of such great points to

</Sent>
<Sent id="2035">
Text = I polished my boots

</Sent>
<Sent id="2036">
Text = I stole the brushes to clean them from

</Sent>
<Sent id="2037">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2038">
Text = I had let myself get too

</Sent>
<Sent id="2039">
Text = My uniform, perhaps, was tidy, but I could not go out to

</Sent>
<Sent id="2040">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2041">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2042">
Text = I knew, too,

</Sent>
<Sent id="2043">
Text = I knew,

</Sent>
<Sent id="2044">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2045">
Text = I could not control myself and was

</Sent>
<Sent id="2046">
Text = With despair I pictured to myself how coldly

commonplace it would all be.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2047">
Text = Of course, the best thing would be not to
go at all.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2048">
Text = But that was most impossible of all: if I feel impelled to do

</Sent>
<Sent id="2049">
Text = I should have jeered at

</Sent>
<Sent id="2050">
Text = What is more, even in the acutest paroxysm of this cowardly

</Sent>
<Sent id="2051">
Text = Then, perhaps, we would be

</Sent>
<Sent id="2052">
Text = Oh, how I

</Sent>
<Sent id="2053">
Text = In unutterable anguish I went to the

</Sent>
<Sent id="2054">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2055">

<Sent id="2056">
Text = IV
</Sent>
<Sent id="2057">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2058">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2748">
Text = With tact and
good-breeding, and, above all, entirely without superfluous words, I
blamed myself for all that had happened.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2749">
Text = I defended myself, "if I really
may be allowed to defend myself," by alleging that being utterly

</Sent>
<Sent id="2750">
Text = I begged Simonov's

</Sent>
<Sent id="2751">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2752">
Text = I added that I would have called upon all of them

</Sent>
<Sent id="2753">
Text = I was

</Sent>
<Sent id="2754">
Text = And it's all because I am an

</Sent>
<Sent id="2755">
Text = Another man in my place would not have

</Sent>
<Sent id="2756">
Text = H'm! ... no, it was not the wine.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2757">
Text = I did not drink anything at

</Sent>
<Sent id="2758">
Text = I had lied to
Simonov;
</Sent>
<Sent id="2759">
Text = I had lied shamelessly; and indeed I wasn't ashamed now....
</Sent>
<Sent id="2760">
Text = Hang it all though, the great thing was that I was rid of it.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2761">
Text = I put six roubles in the letter, sealed it up, and asked Apollon to take

</Sent>
<Sent id="2762">
Text = When he learned that there was money in the letter,
Apollon became more respectful and agreed to take it.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2763">
Text = Towards evening I
went out for a walk.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2764">
Text = My head was still aching and giddy after yesterday.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2765">
Text = But as evening came on and the twilight grew denser, my impressions and,

</Sent>
<Sent id="2766">
Text = Something was not dead within me, in the depths of my heart and

</Sent>
<Sent id="2767">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2768">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2769">
Text = What I liked was just that cheap bustle, that bare prose.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2770">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2771">
Text = I

</Sent>
<Sent id="2772">
Text = I returned home completely upset, it was just

</Sent>
<Sent id="2773">
Text = The thought that Liza was coming worried me continually.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2774">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2775">

</Sent>

</Sent>
<Sent id="2777">
Text = But on this point I

</Sent>
<Sent id="2778">

</Sent>

</Sent>

</Sent>
<Sent id="2781">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2782">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2783">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2784">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2785">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2786">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2787">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2788">

</Sent>

</Sent>
<Sent id="2790">

</Sent>

"Why dishonest?
</Sent>
<Sent id="2793">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2794">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2795">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2796">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2797">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2798">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2799">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2800">
Text = One moment out of all that

</Sent>
<Sent id="2801">
Text = And what a pitiful, what an unnatural, what a distorted smile

</Sent>
<Sent id="2802">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2803">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2804">
Text = I was always

</Sent>
<Sent id="2805">
Text = But, however, "Liza will very likely come all the

</Sent>
<Sent id="2806">
Text = I was so

</Sent>
<Sent id="2807">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2808">

</Sent>

</Sent>
<Sent id="2810">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2811">
Text = And how few, how few words, I thought, in passing, were needed; how

</Sent>
<Sent id="2812">
Text = That's virginity, to be sure!
</Sent>
<Sent id="2813">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2814">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2815">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2816">
Text = I should have insulted her, have spat

</Sent>
<Sent id="2817">
Text = One day passed, however, another and another; she did not come and I

</Sent>
<Sent id="2818">
Text = I felt particularly bold and cheerful after nine

</Sent>
<Sent id="2819">
Text = I develop her, educate her.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2820">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2821">

<Sent id="2822">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2823">
Text = I am amazed, but.... "Liza," I say, "can you

     'Into my house come bold and free,
</Sent>
<Sent id="2824">
Text = Its rightful mistress there to be.'"
</Sent>
<Sent id="2825">
Text = Then we begin living together, go abroad and so on, and so on.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2826">
Text = In fact,
in the end it seemed vulgar to me myself, and I began putting out my

</Sent>
<Sent id="2827">
Text = Besides, they won't let her out, "the hussy!" I thought.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2828">
Text = They don't let

</Sent>
<Sent id="2829">
Text = Though she did say she was not altogether a slave there yet, and had

</Sent>
<Sent id="2830">
Text = Damn it all, she will come, she is sure to

</Sent>
<Sent id="2831">
Text = It was a good thing, in fact, that Apollon distracted my attention at

</Sent>
<Sent id="2832">
Text = He drove me beyond all patience!
</Sent>
<Sent id="2833">
Text = He was the

</Sent>
<Sent id="2834">
Text = We had been

</Sent>
<Sent id="2835">
Text = My God, how I hated

</Sent>
<Sent id="2836">
Text = I believe I had never hated any one in my life as I hated him,

</Sent>
<Sent id="2837">
Text = He was an elderly, dignified man, who worked

</Sent>
<Sent id="2838">
Text = But for some unknown reason he despised me

</Sent>
<Sent id="2839">
Text = Though,

</Sent>
<Sent id="2840">
Text = Simply to glance at that flaxen,

</Sent>
<Sent id="2841">
Text = He was a pedant, to the most extreme point,

</Sent>
<Sent id="2842">
Text = He was in love with every button on his

</Sent>
<Sent id="2843">
Text = In his behaviour to me he was a perfect tyrant, he spoke very

</Sent>
<Sent id="2844">
Text = He did his work with the air of doing me the greatest

</Sent>
<Sent id="2845">
Text = Though he did scarcely anything for me, and did not, indeed,

</Sent>
<Sent id="2846">
Text = There could be no doubt that he

</Sent>
<Sent id="2847">
Text = He

</Sent>
<Sent id="2848">
Text = Many sins
should be forgiven me for what I suffered from him.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2849">
Text = My hatred reached
such a point that sometimes his very step almost threw me into

</Sent>
<Sent id="2850">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2851">
Text = His tongue must
have been a little too long or something of that sort, for he

</Sent>
<Sent id="2852">
Text = He spoke in a slow, measured tone, with

</Sent>
<Sent id="2853">
Text = He maddened

</Sent>
<Sent id="2854">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2855">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2856">
Text = It is interesting that that is how he has

</Sent>
<Sent id="2857">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2858">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2859">
Text = I could not live in furnished lodgings: my lodging was my private

</Sent>
<Sent id="2860">
Text = To be two or three days behind with his wages, for instance, was

</Sent>
<Sent id="2861">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2862">
Text = But I was so exasperated with every one during

</Sent>
<Sent id="2863">
Text = I had for a long time--for the last two years--been

</Sent>
<Sent id="2864">
Text = I purposed to say nothing to him about it, and was purposely

</Sent>
<Sent id="2865">
Text = Then I would take the seven roubles out of

</Sent>
<Sent id="2866">
Text = But angry as I was, yet he got the better of me.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2867">
Text = I could not hold out

</Sent>
<Sent id="2868">
Text = He began as he always did begin in such cases, for there

</Sent>
<Sent id="2869">
Text = He would begin by fixing upon me an exceedingly severe stare, keeping it

</Sent>
<Sent id="2870">
Text = If I held out and pretended not to notice these

</Sent>
<Sent id="2871">
Text = All at

</Sent>
<Sent id="2872">
Text = If I suddenly asked him

</Sent>
<Sent id="2873">
Text = Two hours later he would come out

</Sent>
<Sent id="2874">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2875">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2876">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2877">
Text = This time the usual staring manoeuvres had scarcely begun when I lost

</Sent>
<Sent id="2878">
Text = I was irritated beyond endurance
apart from him.

"Stay," I cried, in a frenzy, as he was slowly and silently turning,

</Sent>
<Sent id="2879">
Text = Come back, come
back, I tell you!" and I must have bawled so unnaturally, that he turned

</Sent>
<Sent id="2880">
Text = However, he persisted in
saying nothing, and that infuriated me.

"How dare you come and look at me like that without being sent for?
</Sent>
<Sent id="2881">
Text = Answer!"
</Sent>
<Sent id="2882">
Text = After looking at me calmly for half a minute, he began turning round

"Stay!" I roared, running up to him, "don't stir!
</Sent>
<Sent id="2883">
Text = There.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2884">
Text = Answer, now:
what did you come in to look at?"

"If you have any order to give me it's my duty to carry it out," he

</Sent>
<Sent id="2885">
Text = He would have turned round again without a word, but I seized him.

</Sent>
<Sent id="2886">
Text = Do you hear?"

"That cannot be," he answered, with the most unnatural self-confidence.

"It shall be so," I said, "I give you my word of honour, it shall be!"

"And there's nothing for me to beg your pardon for," he went on, as

</Sent>
<Sent id="2887">
Text = You are a torturer all the same! a torturer!"
</Sent>
<Sent id="2888">
Text = But he merely looked at me, then turned, and regardless of my loud calls

"If it had not been for Liza nothing of this would have happened," I

</Sent>
<Sent id="2889">
Text = Then, after waiting a minute, I went myself behind his

</Sent>
<Sent id="2890">
Text = He had meanwhile settled himself at his table, put on his spectacles and
taken up some sewing.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2891">
Text = But, hearing my order, he burst into a guffaw.

"At once, go this minute!
</Sent>
<Sent id="2892">
Text = Go on, or else you can't imagine what will
happen."

"You are certainly out of your mind," he observed, without even raising

</Sent>
<Sent id="2893">
Text = And as

</Sent>
<Sent id="2894">
Text = I felt I should strike

</Sent>
<Sent id="2895">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2896">
Text = I glanced, nearly swooned with shame, and rushed back to

</Sent>
<Sent id="2897">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2898">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2899">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2900">

"Go away, go away," I commanded in desperation.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2901">
Text = At that moment my clock
began whirring and wheezing and struck seven.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2902">
Text = IX

     "Into my house come bold and free,
</Sent>
<Sent id="2903">
Text = Its rightful mistress there to be."
</Sent>
<Sent id="2904">
Text = I stood before her crushed, crestfallen, revoltingly confused, and I

</Sent>
<Sent id="2905">
Text = After standing over us for a couple
of minutes Apollon went away, but that did not make me more at ease.
</Sent>
<Sent id="2906">

</Sent>
<Sent id="2907">
Text = At the sight of me, of

"Sit down," I said mechanically, moving a chair up to the table, and I

<Sent id="3043">

</Sent>

</Sent>

</Sent>

</Sent>

</Sent>

</Sent>
<Sent id="3095">

</Sent>

</Sent>

</Sent>

</Sent>

</Sent>
<Sent id="3103">
Text = I longed for that, my whole breast was being

</Sent>
<Sent id="3104">
Text = But--what for?
</Sent>
<Sent id="3105">
Text = I thought.
</Sent>

</Sent>

</Sent>
<Sent id="3108">

</Sent>
<Sent id="3109">
Text = Should I not torture her?
</Sent>

</Sent>

</Sent>

</Sent>

Text = Will all that make things easier for her

</Sent>

</Sent>
<Sent id="3115">
Text = Well, which is better?
</Sent>

</Sent>
<Sent id="3117">
Text = Never had I endured such suffering and remorse, yet could there

</Sent>

Text = I never met Liza again and I have heard

</Sent>
<Sent id="3119">
Text = I will add, too, that I remained for a long time

       *     *     *     *     *
</Sent>
<Sent id="3120">
Text = Even now, so many years later, all this is somehow a very evil memory.
</Sent>
<Sent id="3121">
Text = I
have many evil memories now, but ... hadn't I better end my "Notes"

</Sent>
<Sent id="3122">
Text = I believe I made a mistake in beginning to write them, anyway I
have felt ashamed all the time I've been writing this story; so it's
hardly literature so much as a corrective punishment.
</Sent>
<Sent id="3123">
Text = Why, to tell long
stories, showing how I have spoiled my life through morally rotting in
my corner, through lack of fitting environment, through divorce from
real life, and rankling spite in my underground world, would certainly
not be interesting; a novel needs a hero, and all the traits for an
anti-hero are _expressly_ gathered together here, and what matters most,
it all produces an unpleasant impression, for we are all divorced from
life, we are all cripples, every one of us, more or less.
</Sent>
<Sent id="3124">
Text = We are so
divorced from it that we feel at once a sort of loathing for real life,
and so cannot bear to be reminded of it.
</Sent>
<Sent id="3125">
Text = Why, we have come almost to

privately agreed that it is better in books.
</Sent>
<Sent id="3126">
Text = And why do we fuss and fume
sometimes?
</Sent>
<Sent id="3127">
Text = Why are we perverse and ask for something else?
</Sent>
<Sent id="3128">
Text = We don't know
what ourselves.
</Sent>
<Sent id="3129">
Text = It would be the worse for us if our petulant prayers
were answered.
</Sent>
<Sent id="3130">
Text = Come, try, give any one of us, for instance, a little

relax the control and we ... yes, I assure you ... we should be begging
to be under control again at once.
</Sent>
<Sent id="3131">
Text = I know that you will very likely be
angry with me for that, and will begin shouting and stamping.
</Sent>
<Sent id="3132">
Text = Speak for

</Sent>
<Sent id="3133">
Text = So that perhaps, after all, there is more life in me than in
you.
</Sent>
<Sent id="3134">
Text = Look into it more carefully!
</Sent>
<Sent id="3135">
Text = Why, we don't even know what living

</Sent>
<Sent id="3136">
Text = Leave us alone without
books and we shall be lost and in confusion at once.
</Sent>
<Sent id="3137">
Text = We shall not know

</Sent>
<Sent id="3138">
Text = We are oppressed at being men--men

</Sent>
<Sent id="3139">
Text = We are stillborn, and for generations past have been begotten, not

</Sent>
<Sent id="3140">
Text = We are

</Sent>
<Sent id="3141">

</Sent>
<Sent id="3142">
Text = But enough;
</Sent>
<Sent id="3143">
Text = I don't want to write more from "Underground."

</Sent>
<Sent id="3144">

    to us that we may stop here._]

<Sent id="3145">
Text = A FAINT HEART
</Sent>
<Sent id="3146">
Text = A STORY
</Sent>
<Sent id="3147">
Text = Under the same roof in the same flat on the same fourth storey lived two

</Sent>
<Sent id="3148">
Text = The author of course, feels the necessity of

</Sent>
<Sent id="3149">
Text = But, to do so, it would

</Sent>
<Sent id="3150">
