<Sent id="3981">
Text = There was one who
saw patients.
</Sent>
<Sent id="3982">
Text = This one questioned at length and in detail the servant
who announced that Nefedevitch had called, asking him who it was, from
whom he came, what was the matter, and concluded by saying that he could
not go, that he had a great deal to do, and that patients of that kind
ought to be taken to a hospital.
</Sent>
<Sent id="3983">
Text = Then Arkady, exhausted, agitated, and utterly taken aback by this turn
of affairs, cursed all the doctors on earth, and rushed home in the
utmost alarm about Vasya.
</Sent>
<Sent id="3984">
Text = He ran into the flat.
</Sent>
<Sent id="3985">
Text = Mavra, as though there
were nothing the matter, went on scrubbing the floor, breaking up wood
and preparing to light the stove.
</Sent>
<Sent id="3986">
Text = He went into the room; there was no
trace of Vasya, he had gone out.

"Which way?
</Sent>
<Sent id="3987">
Text = Where?
</Sent>
<Sent id="3988">
Text = Where will the poor fellow be off to?" thought
Arkady, frozen with terror.
</Sent>
<Sent id="3989">
Text = He began questioning Mavra.
</Sent>
<Sent id="3990">
Text = She knew
nothing, had neither seen nor heard him go out, God bless him!
</Sent>
<Sent id="3991">
Text = Nefedevitch rushed off to the Artemyevs'.
</Sent>
<Sent id="3992">
Text = It occurred to him for some reason that he must be there.
</Sent>
<Sent id="3993">
Text = It was ten o'clock by the time he arrived.
</Sent>
<Sent id="3994">
Text = They did not expect him, knew
nothing and had heard nothing.
</Sent>
<Sent id="3995">
Text = He stood before them frightened,
distressed, and asked where was Vasya?
</Sent>
<Sent id="3996">
Text = The mother's legs gave way under
her; she sank back on the sofa.
</Sent>
<Sent id="3997">
Text = Lizanka, trembling with alarm, began
asking what had happened.
</Sent>
<Sent id="3998">
Text = What could he say?
</Sent>
<Sent id="3999">
Text = Arkady Ivanovitch got out
of it as best he could, invented some tale which of course was not
believed, and fled, leaving them distressed and anxious.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4000">
Text = He flew to his
department that he might not be too late there, and he let them know
that steps might be taken at once.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4001">
Text = On the way it occurred to him that
Vasya would be at Yulian Mastakovitch's. That was more likely than
anything: Arkady had thought of that first of all, even before the
Artemyevs'.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4002">
Text = As he drove by His Excellency's door, he thought of
stopping, but at once told the driver to go straight on.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4003">
Text = He made up his
mind to try and find out whether anything had happened at the office,
and if he were not there to go to His Excellency, ostensibly to report
on Vasya.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4004">
Text = Some one must be informed of it.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4005">
Text = As soon as he got into the waiting-room he was surrounded by
fellow-clerks, for the most part young men of his own standing in the
service.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4006">
Text = With one voice they began asking him what had happened to
Vasya?
</Sent>
<Sent id="4007">
Text = At the same time they all told him that Vasya had gone out of his
mind, and thought that he was to be sent for a soldier as a punishment
for having neglected his work.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4008">
Text = Arkady Ivanovitch, answering them in all
directions, or rather avoiding giving a direct answer to any one, rushed
into the inner room.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4009">
Text = On the way he learned that Vasya was in Yulian
Mastakovitch's private room, that every one had been there and that
Esper Ivanovitch had gone in there too.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4010">
Text = He was stopped on the way.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4011">
Text = One
of the senior clerks asked him who he was and what he wanted?
</Sent>
<Sent id="4012">
Text = Without
distinguishing the person he said something about Vasya and went
straight into the room.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4013">
Text = He heard Yulian Mastakovitch's voice from
within. "Where are you going?" some one asked him at the very door.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4014">
Text = Arkady Ivanovitch was almost in despair; he was on the point of turning
back, but through the open door he saw his poor Vasya.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4015">
Text = He pushed the
door and squeezed his way into the room.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4016">
Text = Every one seemed to be in
confusion and perplexity, because Yulian Mastakovitch was apparently
much chagrined.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4017">
Text = All the more important personages were standing about
him talking, and coming to no decision.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4018">
Text = At a little distance stood
Vasya.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4019">
Text = Arkady's heart sank when he looked at him.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4020">
Text = Vasya was standing,
pale, with his head up, stiffly erect, like a recruit before a new
officer, with his feet together and his hands held rigidly at his sides.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4021">
Text = He was looking Yulian Mastakovitch straight in the face.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4022">
Text = Arkady was
noticed at once, and some one who knew that they lodged together
mentioned the fact to His Excellency.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4023">
Text = Arkady was led up to him.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4024">
Text = He tried
to make some answer to the questions put to him, glanced at Yulian
Mastakovitch and seeing on his face a look of genuine compassion, began
trembling and sobbing like a child.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4025">
Text = He even did more, he snatched His
Excellency's hand and held it to his eyes, wetting it with his tears, so
that Yulian Mastakovitch was obliged to draw it hastily away, and waving
it in the air, said, "Come, my dear fellow, come!
</Sent>
<Sent id="4026">
Text = I see you have a good
heart." Arkady sobbed and turned an imploring look on every one.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4027">
Text = It
seemed to him that they were all brothers of his dear Vasya, that they
were all worried and weeping about him. "How, how has it happened? how
has it happened?" asked Yulian Mastakovitch. "What has sent him out of
his mind?"

"Gra--gra--gratitude!" was all Arkady Ivanovitch could articulate.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4028">
Text = Every one heard his answer with amazement, and it seemed strange and
incredible to every one that a man could go out of his mind from
gratitude.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4029">
Text = Arkady explained as best he could.

"Good Heavens! what a pity!" said Yulian Mastakovitch at last. "And the
work entrusted to him was not important, and not urgent in the least.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4030">
Text = It
was not worth while for a man to kill himself over it!
</Sent>
<Sent id="4031">
Text = Well, take him
away!"...
</Sent>
<Sent id="4032">
Text = At this point Yulian Mastakovitch turned to Arkady Ivanovitch
again, and began questioning him once more. "He begs," he said, pointing
to Vasya, "that some girl should not be told of this.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4033">
Text = Who is she--his
betrothed, I suppose?"
</Sent>
<Sent id="4034">
Text = Arkady began to explain.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4035">
Text = Meanwhile Vasya seemed to be thinking of
something, as though he were straining his memory to the utmost to
recall some important, necessary matter, which was particularly wanted
at this moment.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4036">
Text = From time to time he looked round with a distressed
face, as though hoping some one would remind him of what he had
forgotten.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4037">
Text = He fastened his eyes on Arkady.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4038">
Text = All of a sudden there was a
gleam of hope in his eyes; he moved with the left leg forward, took
three steps as smartly as he could, clicking with his right boot as
soldiers do when they move forward at the call from their officer.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4039">
Text = Every
one was waiting to see what would happen.

"I have a physical defect and am small and weak, and I am not fit for
military service, Your Excellency," he said abruptly.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4040">
Text = At that every one in the room felt a pang at his heart, and firm as was
Yulian Mastakovitch's character, tears trickled from his eyes.

"Take him away," he said, with a wave of his hands.

"Present!" said Vasya in an undertone; he wheeled round to the left and
marched out of the room.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4041">
Text = All who were interested in his fate followed
him out.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4042">
Text = Arkady pushed his way out behind the others.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4043">
Text = They made Vasya
sit down in the waiting-room till the carriage came which had been
ordered to take him to the hospital.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4044">
Text = He sat down in silence and seemed
in great anxiety.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4045">
Text = He nodded to any one he recognized as though saying
good-bye.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4046">
Text = He looked round towards the door every minute, and prepared
himself to set off when he should be told it was time.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4047">
Text = People crowded in
a close circle round him; they were all shaking their heads and
lamenting.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4048">
Text = Many of them were much impressed by his story, which had
suddenly become known.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4049">
Text = Some discussed his illness, while others
expressed their pity and high opinion of Vasya, saying that he was such
a quiet, modest young man, that he had been so promising; people
described what efforts he had made to learn, how eager he was for
knowledge, how he had worked to educate himself. "He had risen by his
own efforts from a humble position," some one observed.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4050">
Text = They spoke with
emotion of His Excellency's affection for him.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4051">
Text = Some of them fell to
explaining why Vasya was possessed by the idea that he was being sent
for a soldier, because he had not finished his work.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4052">
Text = They said that the
poor fellow had so lately belonged to the class liable for military
service and had only received his first grade through the good offices
of Yulian Mastakovitch, who had had the cleverness to discover his
talent, his docility, and the rare mildness of his disposition.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4053">
Text = In fact,
there was a great number of views and theories.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4054">
Text = A very short fellow-clerk of Vasya's was conspicuous as being
particularly distressed.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4055">
Text = He was not very young, probably about thirty.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4056">
Text = He was pale as a sheet, trembling all over and smiling queerly, perhaps
because any scandalous affair or terrible scene both frightens, and at
the same time somewhat rejoices the outside spectator.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4057">
Text = He kept running
round the circle that surrounded Vasya, and as he was so short, stood on
tiptoe and caught at the button of every one--that is, of those with
whom he felt entitled to take such a liberty--and kept saying that he
knew how it had all happened, that it was not so simple, but a very
important matter, that it couldn't be left without further inquiry; then
stood on tiptoe again, whispered in some one's ear, nodded his head
again two or three times, and ran round again.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4058">
Text = At last everything was
over.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4059">
Text = The porter made his appearance, and an attendant from the hospital
went up to Vasya and told him it was time to start.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4060">
Text = Vasya jumped up in a
flutter and went with them, looking about him.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4061">
Text = He was looking about for
some one.

"Vasya, Vasya!" cried Arkady Ivanovitch, sobbing.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4062">
Text = Vasya stopped, and
Arkady squeezed his way up to him.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4063">
Text = They flung themselves into each
other's arms in a last bitter embrace.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4064">
Text = It was sad to see them.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4065">
Text = What
monstrous calamity was wringing the tears from their eyes!
</Sent>
<Sent id="4066">
Text = What were
they weeping for?
</Sent>
<Sent id="4067">
Text = What was their trouble?
</Sent>
<Sent id="4068">
Text = Why did they not understand
one another?

"Here, here, take it!
</Sent>
<Sent id="4069">
Text = Take care of it," said Shumkov, thrusting a paper
of some kind into Arkady's hand. "They will take it away from me.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4070">
Text = Bring
it me later on; bring it ... take care of it...." Vasya could not
finish, they called to him.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4071">
Text = He ran hurriedly downstairs, nodding to
every one, saying good-bye to every one.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4072">
Text = There was despair in his face.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4073">
Text = At last he was put in the carriage and taken away.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4074">
Text = Arkady made haste to
open the paper: it was Liza's curl of black hair, from which Vasya had
never parted.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4075">
Text = Hot tears gushed from Arkady's eyes: oh, poor Liza!
</Sent>
<Sent id="4076">
Text = When office hours were over, he went to the Artemyevs'.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4077">
Text = There is no need
to describe what happened there!
</Sent>
<Sent id="4078">
Text = Even Petya, little Petya, though he
could not quite understand what had happened to dear Vasya, went into a
corner, hid his face in his little hands, and sobbed in the fullness of
his childish heart.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4079">
Text = It was quite dusk when Arkady returned home.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4080">
Text = When he
reached the Neva he stood still for a minute and turned a keen glance up
the river into the smoky frozen thickness of the distance, which was
suddenly flushed crimson with the last purple and blood-red glow of
sunset, still smouldering on the misty horizon....
</Sent>
<Sent id="4081">
Text = Night lay over the
city, and the wide plain of the Neva, swollen with frozen snow, was
shining in the last gleams of the sun with myriads of sparks of gleaming
hoar frost.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4082">
Text = There was a frost of twenty degrees.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4083">
Text = A cloud of frozen steam
hung about the overdriven horses and the hurrying people.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4084">
Text = The condensed
atmosphere quivered at the slightest sound, and from all the roofs on
both sides of the river, columns of smoke rose up like giants and
floated across the cold sky, intertwining and untwining as they went, so
that it seemed new buildings were rising up above the old, a new town
was taking shape in the air....
</Sent>
<Sent id="4085">
Text = It seemed as if all that world, with all
its inhabitants, strong and weak, with all their habitations, the
refuges of the poor, or the gilded palaces for the comfort of the
powerful of this world was at that twilight hour like a fantastic vision
of fairy-land, like a dream which in its turn would vanish and pass away
like vapour into the dark blue sky.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4086">
Text = A strange thought came to poor
Vasya's forlorn friend.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4087">
Text = He started, and his heart seemed at that instant
flooded with a hot rush of blood kindled by a powerful, overwhelming
sensation he had never known before.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4088">
Text = He seemed only now to understand
all the trouble, and to know why his poor Vasya had gone out of his
mind, unable to bear his happiness.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4089">
Text = His lips twitched, his eyes lighted
up, he turned pale, and as it were had a clear vision into something
new.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4090">
Text = He became gloomy and depressed, and lost all his gaiety.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4091">
Text = His old lodging
grew hateful to him--he took a new room.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4092">
Text = He did not care to visit the
Artemyevs, and indeed he could not.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4093">
Text = Two years later he met Lizanka in
church.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4094">
Text = She was by then married; beside her walked a wet nurse with a
tiny baby.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4095">
Text = They greeted each other, and for a long time avoided all
mention of the past.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4096">
Text = Liza said that, thank God, she was happy, that she
was not badly off, that her husband was a kind man and that she was fond
of him....
</Sent>
<Sent id="4097">
Text = But suddenly in the middle of a sentence her eyes filled with
tears, her voice failed, she turned away, and bowed down to the church
pavement to hide her grief.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4098">
Text = A CHRISTMAS TREE AND A WEDDING
</Sent>
<Sent id="4099">
Text = A STORY
</Sent>
<Sent id="4100">
Text = The other day I saw a wedding ... but no, I had better tell you about
the Christmas tree.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4101">
Text = The wedding was nice, I liked it very much; but the
other incident was better.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4102">
Text = I don't know how it was that, looking at that
wedding, I thought of that Christmas tree.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4103">
Text = This was what happened.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4104">
Text = Just
five years ago, on New Year's Eve, I was invited to a children's party.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4105">
Text = The giver of the party was a well-known and business-like personage,
with connections, with a large circle of acquaintances, and a good many
schemes on hand, so that it may be supposed that this party was an
excuse for getting the parents together and discussing various
interesting matters in an innocent, casual way.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4106">
Text = I was an outsider;
</Sent>
<Sent id="4107">
Text = I had
no interesting matter to contribute, and so I spent the evening rather
independently.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4108">
Text = There was another gentleman present who was, I fancied,
of no special rank or family, and who, like me, had simply turned up at
this family festivity.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4109">
Text = He was the first to catch my eye.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4110">
Text = He was a tall,
lanky man, very grave and very correctly dressed.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4111">
Text = But one could see that
he was in no mood for merrymaking and family festivity; whenever he
withdrew into a corner he left off smiling and knitted his bushy black
brows.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4112">
Text = He had not a single acquaintance in the party except his host.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4113">
Text = One could see that he was fearfully bored, but that he was valiantly
keeping up the part of a man perfectly happy and enjoying himself.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4114">
Text = I
learned afterwards that this was a gentleman from the provinces, who had
a critical and perplexing piece of business in Petersburg, who had
brought a letter of introduction to our host, for whom our host was, by
no means _con amore_, using his interest, and whom he had invited, out
of civility, to his children's party.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4115">
Text = He did not play cards, cigars were
not offered him, every one avoided entering into conversation with him,
most likely recognizing the bird from its feathers; and so my gentleman
was forced to sit the whole evening stroking his whiskers simply to have
something to do with his hands.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4116">
Text = His whiskers were certainly very fine.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4117">
Text = But he stroked them so zealously that, looking at him, one might have
supposed that the whiskers were created first and the gentleman only
attached to them in order to stroke them.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4118">
Text = In addition to this individual who assisted in this way at our host's
family festivity (he had five fat, well-fed boys), I was attracted, too,
by another gentleman.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4119">
Text = But he was quite of a different sort.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4120">
Text = He was a
personage.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4121">
Text = He was called Yulian Mastakovitch.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4122">
Text = From the first glance one
could see that he was an honoured guest, and stood in the same relation
to our host as our host stood in relation to the gentleman who was
stroking his whiskers.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4123">
Text = Our host and hostess said no end of polite things
to him, waited on him hand and foot, pressed him to drink, flattered
him, brought their visitors up to be introduced to him, but did not take
him to be introduced to any one else.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4124">
Text = I noticed that tears glistened in
our host's eyes when he remarked about the party that he had rarely
spent an evening so agreeably.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4125">
Text = I felt as it were frightened in the
presence of such a personage, and so, after admiring the children, I
went away into a little parlour, which was quite empty, and sat down in
an arbour of flowers which filled up almost half the room.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4126">
Text = The children were all incredibly sweet, and resolutely refused to model
themselves on the "grown-ups," regardless of all the admonitions of
their governesses and mammas.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4127">
Text = They stripped the Christmas tree to the
last sweetmeat in the twinkling of an eye, and had succeeded in breaking
half the playthings before they knew what was destined for which.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4128">
Text = Particularly charming was a black-eyed, curly-headed boy, who kept
trying to shoot me with his wooden gun.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4129">
Text = But my attention was still more
attracted by his sister, a girl of eleven, quiet, dreamy, pale, with
big, prominent, dreamy eyes, exquisite as a little Cupid.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4130">
Text = The children
hurt her feelings in some way, and so she came away from them to the
same empty parlour in which I was sitting, and played with her doll in
the corner.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4131">
Text = The visitors respectfully pointed out her father, a wealthy
contractor, and some one whispered that three hundred thousand roubles
were already set aside for her dowry.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4132">
Text = I turned round to glance at the
group who were interested in such a circumstance, and my eye fell on
Yulian Mastakovitch, who, with his hands behind his back and his head on
one side, was listening with the greatest attention to these gentlemen's
idle gossip.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4133">
Text = Afterwards I could not help admiring the discrimination of
the host and hostess in the distribution of the children's presents.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4134">
Text = The
little girl, who had already a portion of three hundred thousand
roubles, received the costliest doll.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4135">
Text = Then followed presents diminishing
in value in accordance with the rank of the parents of these happy
children; finally, the child of lowest degree, a thin, freckled,
red-haired little boy of ten, got nothing but a book of stories about
the marvels of nature and tears of devotion, etc., without pictures or
even woodcuts.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4136">
Text = He was the son of a poor widow, the governess of the
children of the house, an oppressed and scared little boy.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4137">
Text = He was
dressed in a short jacket of inferior nankin.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4138">
Text = After receiving his book
he walked round the other toys for a long time; he longed to play with
the other children, but did not dare; it was evident that he already
felt and understood his position.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4139">
Text = I love watching children.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4140">
Text = Their first
independent approaches to life are extremely interesting.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4141">
Text = I noticed that
the red-haired boy was so fascinated by the costly toys of the other
children, especially by a theatre in which he certainly longed to take
some part, that he made up his mind to sacrifice his dignity.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4142">
Text = He smiled
and began playing with the other children, he gave away his apple to a
fat-faced little boy who had a mass of goodies tied up in a
pocket-handkerchief already, and even brought himself to carry another
boy on his back, simply not to be turned away from the theatre, but an
insolent youth gave him a heavy thump a minute later.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4143">
Text = The child did not
dare to cry.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4144">
Text = Then the governess, his mother, made her appearance, and
told him not to interfere with the other children's playing.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4145">
Text = The boy
went away to the same room in which was the little girl.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4146">
Text = She let him
join her, and the two set to work very eagerly dressing the expensive
doll.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4147">
Text = I had been sitting more than half an hour in the ivy arbour, listening
to the little prattle of the red-haired boy and the beauty with the
dowry of three hundred thousand, who was nursing her doll, when Yulian
Mastakovitch suddenly walked into the room.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4148">
Text = He had taken advantage of
the general commotion following a quarrel among the children to step out
of the drawing-room.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4149">
Text = I had noticed him a moment before talking very
cordially to the future heiress's papa, whose acquaintance he had just
made, of the superiority of one branch of the service over another.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4150">
Text = Now
he stood in hesitation and seemed to be reckoning something on his
fingers.

<Sent id="4239">
Text = POLZUNKOV
</Sent>
<Sent id="4240">
Text = A STORY
</Sent>
<Sent id="4241">
Text = I began to scrutinize the man closely.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4242">
Text = Even in his exterior there was
something so peculiar that it compelled one, however far away one's

</Sent>
<Sent id="4243">
Text = That is what happened to me.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4244">
Text = I must
observe that the little man's eyes were so mobile, or perhaps he was so
sensitive to the magnetism of every eye fixed upon him, that he almost
by instinct guessed that he was being observed, turned at once to the
observer and anxiously analysed his expression.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4245">
Text = His continual mobility,
his turning and twisting, made him look strikingly like a dancing doll.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4246">
Text = It was strange!
</Sent>
<Sent id="4247">
Text = He seemed afraid of jeers, in spite of the fact that he
was almost getting his living by being a buffoon for all the world, and
exposed himself to every buffet in a moral sense and even in a physical
one, judging from the company he was in.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4248">
Text = Voluntary buffoons are not even
to be pitied.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4249">
Text = But I noticed at once that this strange creature, this
ridiculous man, was by no means a buffoon by profession.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4250">
Text = There was still
something gentlemanly in him.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4251">
Text = His very uneasiness, his continual
apprehensiveness about himself, were actually a testimony in his favour.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4252">
Text = It seemed to me that his desire to be obliging was due more to kindness
of heart than to mercenary considerations.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4253">
Text = He readily allowed them to
laugh their loudest at him and in the most unseemly way, to his face,
but at the same time--and I am ready to take my oath on it--his heart
ached and was sore at the thought that his listeners were so caddishly
brutal as to be capable of laughing, not at anything said or done, but
at him, at his whole being, at his heart, at his head, at his
appearance, at his whole body, flesh and blood.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4254">
Text = I am convinced that he
felt at that moment all the foolishness of his position; but the protest
died away in his heart at once, though it invariably sprang up again in
the most heroic way.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4255">
Text = I am convinced that all this was due to nothing
else but a kind heart, and not to fear of the inconvenience of being
kicked out and being unable to borrow money from some one.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4256">
Text = This
gentleman was for ever borrowing money, that is, he asked for alms in
that form, when after playing the fool and entertaining them at his
expense he felt in a certain sense entitled to borrow money from them.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4257">
Text = But, good heavens! what a business the borrowing was!
</Sent>
<Sent id="4258">
Text = And with what a
countenance he asked for the loan!
</Sent>
<Sent id="4259">
Text = I could not have imagined that on
such a small space as the wrinkled, angular face of that little man room
could be found, at one and the same time, for so many different

such absolutely killing expressions.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4260">
Text = Everything was there--shame and an
assumption of insolence, and vexation at the sudden flushing of his

having dared to pester, and a sense of his own dignity, and a still
greater sense of his own abjectness--all this passed over his face like
lightning.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4261">
Text = For six whole years he had struggled along in God's world in
this way, and so far had been unable to take up a fitting attitude at
the interesting moment of borrowing money!
</Sent>
<Sent id="4262">
Text = I need not say that he never
could grow callous and completely abject.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4263">
Text = His heart was too sensitive,
too passionate!
</Sent>
<Sent id="4264">
Text = I will say more, indeed: in my opinion, he was one of

</Sent>
<Sent id="4265">
Text = In short, he was what is called "a rag" in the fullest sense of the

</Sent>
<Sent id="4266">

</Sent>
<Sent id="4267">

</Sent>
<Sent id="4268">

</Sent>
<Sent id="4269">
Text = But equality he could

<Sent id="4271">

          "'Oh, may I be a Liberal,

</Sent>

</Sent>
<Sent id="4306">

</Sent>

Text = I put in my pocket a bribe

"So then he bought them from you?"

"He did."

"He gave as much as many a man nowadays would sell his conscience for

</Sent>
<Sent id="4308">
Text = But I felt as though I were scalded when I put the money in my

</Sent>

</Sent>

</Sent>

</Sent>

</Sent>

</Sent>

Text = Fedosey Nikolaitch

"'Oh no,' he said. 'God will be merciful, He will not visit my
transgressions on you.'

"Then he dismissed them all, told me to shut the door after them, and we
were left alone, _tête-à-tête_.

"'I have a favour to ask of you.'

"'What favour?'

"'Well, my dear boy, there is no rest for me even on my deathbed.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4469">
Text = I am
in want.'

"'How so?' I positively flushed crimson, I could hardly speak.

"'Why, I had to pay some of my own money into the Treasury.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4470">
Text = I grudge
nothing for the public weal, my boy!
</Sent>
<Sent id="4471">
Text = I don't grudge my life.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4472">
Text = Don't you
imagine any ill.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4473">
Text = I am sad to think that slanderers have blackened my
name to you....
</Sent>
<Sent id="4474">
Text = You were mistaken, my hair has gone white from grief.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4475">
Text = The Inspector is coming down upon us and Matveyev is seven thousand
roubles short, and I shall have to answer for it....
</Sent>
<Sent id="4476">
Text = Who else?
</Sent>
<Sent id="4477">
Text = It will
be visited upon me, my boy: where were my eyes?
</Sent>
<Sent id="4478">
Text = And how can we get it
from Matveyev?
</Sent>
<Sent id="4479">
Text = He has had trouble enough already: why should I bring the
poor fellow to ruin?'

"'Holy saints!' I thought, 'what a just man!
</Sent>
<Sent id="4480">
Text = What a heart!'

"'And I don't want to take my daughter's money, which has been set aside
for her dowry: that sum is sacred.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4481">
Text = I have money of my own, it's true,
but I have lent it all to friends--how is one to collect it all in a

"I simply fell on my knees before him. 'My benefactor!' I cried, 'I've
wronged you, I have injured you; it was slanderers who wrote against

"He looked at me and there were tears in his eyes. 'That was just what I
expected from you, my son.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4482">
Text = Get up!
</Sent>
<Sent id="4483">
Text = I forgave you at the time for the
sake of my daughter's tears--now my heart forgives you freely!
</Sent>
<Sent id="4484">
Text = You have
healed my wounds.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4485">
Text = I bless you for all time!'

"Well, when he blessed me, gentlemen, I scurried home as soon as I
could.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4486">
Text = I got the money:

"'Here, father, here's the money.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4487">
Text = I've only spent fifty roubles.'

"'Well, that's all right,' he said. 'But now every trifle may count; the
time is short, write a report dated some days ago that you were short of
money and had taken fifty roubles on account.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4488">
Text = I'll tell the authorities
you had it in advance.'

"Well, gentlemen, what do you think?
</Sent>
<Sent id="4489">
Text = I did write that report, too!"

"Well, what then?
</Sent>
<Sent id="4490">
Text = What happened?
</Sent>
<Sent id="4491">
Text = How did it end?"

"As soon as I had written the report, gentlemen, this is how it ended.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4492">
Text = The next day, in the early morning, an envelope with a government seal
arrived.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4493">
Text = I looked at it and what had I got?
</Sent>
<Sent id="4494">
Text = The sack!
</Sent>
<Sent id="4495">
Text = That is,
instructions to hand over my work, to deliver the accounts--and to go
about my business!"

"How so?"

"That's just what I cried at the top of my voice, 'How so?' Gentlemen,
there was a ringing in my ears.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4496">
Text = I thought there was no special reason
for it--but no, the Inspector had arrived in the town.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4497">
Text = My heart sank.
'It's not for nothing,' I thought.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4498">
Text = And just as I was I rushed off to
Fedosey Nikolaitch.

"'How is this?' I said.

"'What do you mean?' he said.

"'Why, I am dismissed.'

"'Dismissed? how?'

"'Why, look at this!'

"'Well, what of it?'

"'Why, but I didn't ask for it!'

"'Yes, you did--you sent in your papers on the first of--April.' (I had
never taken that letter back!)

"'Fedosey Nikolaitch!
</Sent>
<Sent id="4499">
Text = I can't believe my ears, I can't believe my eyes!
</Sent>
<Sent id="4500">
Text = Is this you?'

"'It is me, why?'

"'My God!'

"'I am sorry, sir.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4501">
Text = I am very sorry that you made up your mind to retire
from the service so early.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4502">
Text = A young man ought to be in the service, and
you've begun to be a little light-headed of late.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4503">
Text = And as for your

</Sent>
<Sent id="4504">
Text = Your behaviour has
always been so exemplary!'

"'But that was a little joke, Fedosey Nikolaitch!
</Sent>
<Sent id="4505">
Text = I didn't mean it, I

"'That's all?
</Sent>
<Sent id="4506">
Text = A queer joke, sir!
</Sent>
<Sent id="4507">
Text = Does one jest with documents like that?
</Sent>
<Sent id="4508">
Text = Why, you are sometimes sent to Siberia for such jokes.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4509">
Text = Now, good-bye.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4510">
Text = I
am busy.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4511">
Text = We have the Inspector here--the duties of the service before
everything; you can kick up your heels, but we have to sit here at work.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4512">

</Sent>
<Sent id="4513">
Text = We are moving in in a day or two.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4514">
Text = So I expect I
shall not have the pleasure of seeing you at our new residence. _Bon

"I ran home.

"'We are lost, granny!'

"She wailed, poor dear, and then I saw the page from Fedosey

</Sent>
<Sent id="4515">
Text = In the fullness of my heart I had given her the

</Sent>
<Sent id="4516">
Text = And in the note there were the words: 'April 1st,' and nothing

</Sent>
<Sent id="4517">
Text = What do you think of that, gentlemen?"

"What happened then?
</Sent>
<Sent id="4518">
Text = What happened then?"

"What then!
</Sent>
<Sent id="4519">
Text = I met Fedosey Nikolaitch once, I meant to tell him to his
face he was a scoundrel."

"Well?"

"But somehow I couldn't bring myself to it, gentlemen."
</Sent>
<Sent id="4520">
Text = A LITTLE HERO
</Sent>
<Sent id="4521">
Text = A STORY
</Sent>
<Sent id="4522">
Text = At that time I was nearly eleven, I had been sent in July to spend the
holiday in a village near Moscow with a relation of mine called T.,
whose house was full of guests, fifty, or perhaps more....
</Sent>
<Sent id="4523">
Text = I don't
remember, I didn't count.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4524">
Text = The house was full of noise and gaiety.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4525">
Text = It
seemed as though it were a continual holiday, which would never end.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4526">
Text = It
seemed as though our host had taken a vow to squander all his vast
fortune as rapidly as possible, and he did indeed succeed, not long ago,
in justifying this surmise, that is, in making a clean sweep of it all
to the last stick.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4527">
Text = Fresh visitors used to drive up every minute.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4528">
Text = Moscow was close by, in
sight, so that those who drove away only made room for others, and the
everlasting holiday went on its course.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4529">
Text = Festivities succeeded one
another, and there was no end in sight to the entertainments.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4530">
Text = There were
riding parties about the environs; excursions to the forest or the
river; picnics, dinners in the open air; suppers on the great terrace of
the house, bordered with three rows of gorgeous flowers that flooded
with their fragrance the fresh night air, and illuminated the brilliant
lights which made our ladies, who were almost every one of them pretty
at all times, seem still more charming, with their faces excited by the
impressions of the day, with their sparkling eyes, with their
interchange of spritely conversation, their peals of ringing laughter;
dancing, music, singing; if the sky were overcast tableaux vivants,
charades, proverbs were arranged, private theatricals were got up.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4531">
Text = There
were good talkers, story-tellers, wits.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4532">
Text = Certain persons were prominent in the foreground.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4533">
Text = Of course backbiting
and slander ran their course, as without them the world could not get
on, and millions of persons would perish of boredom, like flies.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4534">
Text = But as
I was at that time eleven I was absorbed by very different interests,
and either failed to observe these people, or if I noticed anything, did
not see it all.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4535">
Text = It was only afterwards that some things came back to my
mind.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4536">
Text = My childish eyes could only see the brilliant side of the picture,
and the general animation, splendour, and bustle--all that, seen and
heard for the first time, made such an impression upon me that for the
first few days, I was completely bewildered and my little head was in a
whirl.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4537">
Text = I keep speaking of my age, and of course I was a child, nothing more
than a child.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4538">
Text = Many of these lovely ladies petted me without dreaming of
considering my age.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4539">
Text = But strange to say, a sensation which I did not
myself understand already had possession of me; something was already
whispering in my heart, of which till then it had had no knowledge, no
conception, and for some reason it began all at once to burn and throb,
and often my face glowed with a sudden flush.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4540">
Text = At times I felt as it were
abashed, and even resentful of the various privileges of my childish
years.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4541">
Text = At other times a sort of wonder overwhelmed me, and I would go
off into some corner where I could sit unseen, as though to take breath
and remember something--something which it seemed to me I had remembered
perfectly till then, and now had suddenly forgotten, something without
which I could not show myself anywhere, and could not exist at all.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4542">
Text = At last it seemed to me as though I were hiding something from every
one.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4543">
Text = But nothing would have induced me to speak of it to any one,
because, small boy that I was, I was ready to weep with shame.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4544">
Text = Soon in
the midst of the vortex around me I was conscious of a certain
loneliness.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4545">
Text = There were other children, but all were either much older or
younger than I; besides, I was in no mood for them.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4546">
Text = Of course nothing
would have happened to me if I had not been in an exceptional position.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4547">
Text = In the eyes of those charming ladies I was still the little unformed
creature whom they at once liked to pet, and with whom they could play
as though he were a little doll.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4548">
Text = One of them particularly, a
fascinating, fair woman, with very thick luxuriant hair, such as I had
never seen before and probably shall never see again, seemed to have
taken a vow never to leave me in peace.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4549">
Text = I was confused, while she was
amused by the laughter which she continually provoked from all around us
by her wild, giddy pranks with me, and this apparently gave her immense
enjoyment.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4550">
Text = At school among her schoolfellows she was probably nicknamed
the Tease.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4551">
Text = She was wonderfully good-looking, and there was something in
her beauty which drew one's eyes from the first moment.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4552">
Text = And certainly
she had nothing in common with the ordinary modest little fair girls,
white as down and soft as white mice, or pastors' daughters.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4553">
Text = She was not
very tall, and was rather plump, but had soft, delicate, exquisitely cut
features.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4554">
Text = There was something quick as lightning in her face, and indeed
she was like fire all over, light, swift, alive.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4555">
Text = Her big open eyes
seemed to flash sparks; they glittered like diamonds, and I would never
exchange such blue sparkling eyes for any black ones, were they blacker
than any Andalusian orb.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4556">
Text = And, indeed, my blonde was fully a match for
the famous brunette whose praises were sung by a great and well-known
poet, who, in a superb poem, vowed by all Castille that he was ready to
break his bones to be permitted only to touch the mantle of his divinity
with the tip of his finger.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4557">
Text = Add to that, that _my_ charmer was the
merriest in the world, the wildest giggler, playful as a child, although
she had been married for the last five years.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4558">
Text = There was a continual
laugh upon her lips, fresh as the morning rose that, with the first ray
of sunshine, opens its fragrant crimson bud with the cool dewdrops still
hanging heavy upon it.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4559">
Text = I remember that the day after my arrival private theatricals were being
got up.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4560">
Text = The drawing-room was, as they say, packed to overflowing; there
was not a seat empty, and as I was somehow late I had to enjoy the
performance standing.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4561">
Text = But the amusing play attracted me to move
forwarder and forwarder, and unconsciously I made my way to the first
row, where I stood at last leaning my elbows on the back of an armchair,
in which a lady was sitting.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4562">
Text = It was my blonde divinity, but we had not
yet made acquaintance.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4563">
Text = And I gazed, as it happened, at her marvellous,
fascinating shoulders, plump and white as milk, though it did not matter
to me in the least whether I stared at a woman's exquisite shoulders or
at the cap with flaming ribbons that covered the grey locks of a
venerable lady in the front row.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4564">
Text = Near my blonde divinity sat a spinster
lady not in her first youth, one of those who, as I chanced to observe
later, always take refuge in the immediate neighbourhood of young and
pretty women, selecting such as are not fond of cold-shouldering young
men.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4565">
Text = But that is not the point, only this lady, noting my fixed gaze,
bent down to her neighbour and with a simper whispered something in her
ear.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4566">
Text = The blonde lady turned at once, and I remember that her glowing
eyes so flashed upon me in the half dark, that, not prepared to meet
them, I started as though I were scalded.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4567">
Text = The beauty smiled.

"Do you like what they are acting?" she asked, looking into my face with
a shy and mocking expression.

"Yes," I answered, still gazing at her with a sort of wonder that
evidently pleased her.

"But why are you standing?
</Sent>
<Sent id="4568">
Text = You'll get tired.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4569">
Text = Can't you find a seat?"

"That's just it, I can't," I answered, more occupied with my grievance
than with the beauty's sparkling eyes, and rejoicing in earnest at
having found a kind heart to whom I could confide my troubles. "I have
looked everywhere, but all the chairs are taken," I added, as though
complaining to her that all the chairs were taken.

"Come here," she said briskly, quick to act on every decision, and,
indeed, on every mad idea that flashed on her giddy brain, "come here,
and sit on my knee."

"On your knee," I repeated, taken aback.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4570">
Text = I have mentioned already that I
had begun to resent the privileges of childhood and to be ashamed of
them in earnest.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4571">
Text = This lady, as though in derision, had gone ever so much
further than the others.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4572">
Text = Moreover, I had always been a shy and bashful
boy, and of late had begun to be particularly shy with women.

"Why yes, on my knee.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4573">
Text = Why don't you want to sit on my knee?" she
persisted, beginning to laugh more and more, so that at last she was
simply giggling, goodness knows at what, perhaps at her freak, or
perhaps at my confusion.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4574">
Text = But that was just what she wanted.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4575">
Text = I flushed, and in my confusion looked round trying to find where to
escape; but seeing my intention she managed to catch hold of my hand to
prevent me from going away, and pulling it towards her, suddenly, quite
unexpectedly, to my intense astonishment, squeezed it in her mischievous
warm fingers, and began to pinch my fingers till they hurt so much that
I had to do my very utmost not to cry out, and in my effort to control
myself made the most absurd grimaces.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4576">
Text = I was, besides, moved to the
greatest amazement, perplexity, and even horror, at the discovery that
there were ladies so absurd and spiteful as to talk nonsense to boys,
and even pinch their fingers, for no earthly reason and before
everybody.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4577">
Text = Probably my unhappy face reflected my bewilderment, for the
mischievous creature laughed in my face, as though she were crazy, and
meantime she was pinching my fingers more and more vigorously.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4578">
Text = She was
highly delighted in playing such a mischievous prank and completely
mystifying and embarrassing a poor boy.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4579">
Text = My position was desperate.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4580">
Text = In
the first place I was hot with shame, because almost every one near had
turned round to look at us, some in wonder, others with laughter,
grasping at once that the beauty was up to some mischief.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4581">
Text = I dreadfully
wanted to scream, too, for she was wringing my fingers with positive
fury just because I didn't scream; while I, like a Spartan, made up my
mind to endure the agony, afraid by crying out of causing a general
fuss, which was more than I could face.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4582">
Text = In utter despair I began at last
struggling with her, trying with all my might to pull away my hand, but
my persecutor was much stronger than I was.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4583">
Text = At last I could bear it no
longer, and uttered a shriek--that was all she was waiting for!
</Sent>
<Sent id="4584">
Text = Instantly she let me go, and turned away as though nothing had happened,
as though it was not she who had played the trick but some one else,
exactly like some schoolboy who, as soon as the master's back is turned,
plays some trick on some one near him, pinches some small weak boy,
gives him a flip, a kick, or a nudge with his elbows, and instantly
turns again, buries himself in his book and begins repeating his lesson,
and so makes a fool of the infuriated teacher who flies down like a hawk
at the noise.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4585">
Text = But luckily for me the general attention was distracted at the moment by
the masterly acting of our host, who was playing the chief part in the
performance, some comedy of Scribe's. Every one began to applaud; under
cover of the noise I stole away and hurried to the furthest end of the
room, from which, concealed behind a column, I looked with horror
towards the place where the treacherous beauty was sitting.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4586">
Text = She was
still laughing, holding her handkerchief to her lips.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4587">
Text = And for a long
time she was continually turning round, looking for me in every
direction, probably regretting that our silly tussle was so soon over,
and hatching some other trick to play on me.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4588">
Text = That was the beginning of our acquaintance, and from that evening she
would never let me alone.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4589">
Text = She persecuted me without consideration or
conscience, she became my tyrant and tormentor.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4590">
Text = The whole absurdity of
her jokes with me lay in the fact that she pretended to be head over
ears in love with me, and teased me before every one.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4591">
Text = Of course for a
wild creature as I was all this was so tiresome and vexatious that it
almost reduced me to tears, and I was sometimes put in such a difficult
position that I was on the point of fighting with my treacherous
admirer.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4592">
Text = My naïve confusion, my desperate distress, seemed to egg her on
to persecute me more; she knew no mercy, while I did not know how to get
away from her.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4593">
Text = The laughter which always accompanied us, and which she
knew so well how to excite, roused her to fresh pranks.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4594">
Text = But at last
people began to think that she went a little too far in her jests.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4595">
Text = And,
indeed, as I remember now, she did take outrageous liberties with a
child such as I was.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4596">
Text = But that was her character; she was a spoilt child in every respect.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4597">
Text = I
heard afterwards that her husband, a very short, very fat, and very
red-faced man, very rich and apparently very much occupied with
business, spoilt her more than any one.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4598">
Text = Always busy and flying round, he
could not stay two hours in one place.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4599">
Text = Every day he drove into Moscow,
sometimes twice in the day, and always, as he declared himself, on
business.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4600">
Text = It would be hard to find a livelier and more good-natured face
than his facetious but always well-bred countenance.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4601">
Text = He not only loved
his wife to the point of weakness, softness: he simply worshipped her
like an idol.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4602">
Text = He did not restrain her in anything.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4603">
Text = She had masses of friends, male and
female.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4604">
Text = In the first place, almost everybody liked her; and secondly,
the feather-headed creature was not herself over particular in the
choice of her friends, though there was a much more serious foundation
to her character than might be supposed from what I have just said about
her.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4605">
Text = But of all her friends she liked best of all one young lady, a
distant relation, who was also of our party now.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4606">
Text = There existed between
them a tender and subtle affection, one of those attachments which
sometimes spring up at the meeting of two dispositions often the very
opposite of each other, of which one is deeper, purer and more austere,
while the other, with lofty humility, and generous self-criticism,
lovingly gives way to the other, conscious of the friend's superiority
and cherishing the friendship as a happiness.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4607">
Text = Then begins that tender
and noble subtlety in the relations of such characters, love and
infinite indulgence on the one side, on the other love and respect--a
respect approaching awe, approaching anxiety as to the impression made
on the friend so highly prized, and an eager, jealous desire to get
closer and closer to that friend's heart in every step in life.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4608">
Text = These two friends were of the same age, but there was an immense
difference between them in everything--in looks, to begin with.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4609">
Text = Madame
M. was also very handsome, but there was something special in her beauty
that strikingly distinguished her from the crowd of pretty women; there
was something in her face that at once drew the affection of all to her,
or rather, which aroused a generous and lofty feeling of kindliness in
every one who met her.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4610">
Text = There are such happy faces.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4611">
Text = At her side everyone
grew as it were better, freer, more cordial; and yet her big mournful
eyes, full of fire and vigour, had a timid and anxious look, as though
every minute dreading something antagonistic and menacing, and this
strange timidity at times cast so mournful a shade over her mild, gentle
features which recalled the serene faces of Italian Madonnas, that
looking at her one soon became oneself sad, as though for some trouble
of one's own.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4612">
Text = The pale, thin face, in which, through the irreproachable
beauty of the pure, regular lines and the mournful severity of some mute
hidden grief, there often flitted the clear looks of early childhood,
telling of trustful years and perhaps simple-hearted happiness in the
recent past, the gentle but diffident, hesitating smile, all aroused
such unaccountable sympathy for her that every heart was unconsciously
stirred with a sweet and warm anxiety that powerfully interceded on her
behalf even at a distance, and made even strangers feel akin to her.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4613">
Text = But
the lovely creature seemed silent and reserved, though no one could have
been more attentive and loving if any one needed sympathy.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4614">
Text = There are
women who are like sisters of mercy in life.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4615">
Text = Nothing can be hidden from
them, nothing, at least, that is a sore or wound of the heart.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4616">
Text = Any one

<Sent id="4617">

<Sent id="4666">
Text = He was called a _clever man_.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4667">
Text = Such is the name given in certain circles to a peculiar species of
mankind which grows fat at other people's expense, which does absolutely
nothing and has no desire to do anything, and whose heart has turned
into a lump of fat from everlasting slothfulness and idleness.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4668">
Text = You
continually hear from such men that there is nothing they can do owing
to certain very complicated and hostile circumstances, which "thwart
their genius," and that it was "sad to see the waste of their talents."
This is a fine phrase of theirs, their _mot d'ordre_, their watchword, a
phrase which these well-fed, fat friends of ours bring out at every
minute, so that it has long ago bored us as an arrant Tartuffism, an
empty form of words.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4669">
Text = Some, however, of these amusing creatures, who
cannot succeed in finding anything to do--though, indeed, they never
seek it--try to make every one believe that they have not a lump of fat
for a heart, but on the contrary, something _very deep_, though what
precisely the greatest surgeon would hardly venture to decide--from
civility, of course.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4670">
Text = These gentlemen make their way in the world through
the fact that all their instincts are bent in the direction of coarse

</Sent>
<Sent id="4671">
Text = Since they have
nothing else to do but note and emphasize the mistakes and weaknesses of

on with people fairly successfully.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4672">
Text = They pride themselves extremely upon
that.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4673">
Text = They are, for instance, as good as persuaded that almost the whole
world owes them something; that it is theirs, like an oyster which they
keep in reserve; that all are fools except themselves; that every one is
like an orange or a sponge, which they will squeeze as soon as they want
the juice; that they are the masters everywhere, and that all this
acceptable state of affairs is solely due to the fact that they are
people of so much intellect and character.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4674">
Text = In their measureless conceit
they do not admit any defects in themselves, they are like that species
of practical rogues, innate Tartuffes and Falstaffs, who are such
thorough rogues that at last they have come to believe that that is as
it should be, that is, that they should spend their lives in
knavishness; they have so often assured every one that they are honest
men, that they have come to believe that they are honest men, and that
their roguery is honesty.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4675">
Text = They are never capable of inner judgment

they are too fat.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4676">
Text = Their own priceless personality, their Baal and
Moloch, their magnificent _ego_ is always in their foreground

</Sent>
<Sent id="4677">
Text = All nature, the whole world for them is no more than a
splendid mirror created for the little god to admire himself continually

strange that he sees everything in the world in such a hideous light.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4678">
Text = He

</Sent>
<Sent id="4679">
Text = It is just these people, indeed,

which they scent success.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4680">
Text = A fine nose is just what they have for
sniffing a fashionable phrase and making it their own before other
people get hold of it, so that it seems to have originated with them.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4681">
Text = They have a particular store of phrases for proclaiming their profound
sympathy for humanity, for defining what is the most correct and
rational form of philanthropy, and continually attacking romanticism, in
other words, everything fine and true, each atom of which is more
precious than all their mollusc tribe.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4682">
Text = But they are too coarse to

</Sent>
<Sent id="4683">
Text = The well-nourished man has spent all his life in merry-making, with

every sort of work is, and so woe betide you if you jar upon his fat
feelings by any sort of roughness; he'll never forgive you for that, he
will always remember it and will gladly avenge it.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4684">
Text = The long and short of

<Sent id="4685">

</Sent>
<Sent id="4773">
Text = I don't know for certain, but I fancy the whole proceeding was not

</Sent>
<Sent id="4774">
Text = This desperate duel had begun earlier, at

</Sent>
<Sent id="4775">
Text = I call it desperate because M. M. was not quick to surrender.
</Sent>
<Sent id="4776">

</Sent>

Text = Overwhelmed

</Sent>
